Inhalation Toxicology
anesthetized animal does not breathe spontaneously but fol-
lows the external breathing pattern triggered by the pressure in 
the plethysmograph chamber (Kreyling et al. 2002, Semmler-
Behnke et al. 2012). Since the entire aerosol system is main-
tained at a slightly lower pressure of 20–30 Pa compared to the 
lab pressure, the use of radioactively labeled, freshly gener-
ated NP aerosols is possible, with radiation protection of the 
operating personnel. Various aerosolized materials (iridium, 
elemental carbon, titanium dioxide, gold, and silver) with a 
median diameter of 20 nm have been used in biokinetic studies 
(Kreyling et al. 2009, 2011, Möller et al. 2013) (Figure 30.9).
30.3.3  Atmosphere Analysis: Measuring 
Concentration and Particle Size
In many cases, analysis of the atmospheres used is the most 
challenging part of conducting inhalation studies. The inves-
tigator must not only monitor the chamber for proper envi-
ronmental conditions (temperature, relative humidity, oxygen 
content, animal waste accumulation, and airflow/airflow dis-
tribution within the chamber) but must also select, validate, 
and conduct analyses for the test substance(s) in terms of par-
ticle size (if a solid/liquid aerosol), concentration, and solvent 
concentration (if present in a mixture). These challenges are 
magnified when test materials are not stable in air or in the 
presence of moisture and multiple component substances are 
tested. All such issues must be addressed and resolved for 
the study to be considered well-conducted. This section aims 
to point out some of the available analytical approaches and 
methods that may be useful.
Analysis of test atmospheres begins with the sampling sys-
tem. An appropriately designed sampling system is key to 
accurate analysis of test substance concentrations accurately 
in the exposure chamber. Initial studies are needed with the 
chamber outfitted internally as it will be during the inhalation 
experiments to determine how the aerosol in the chamber will 
be distributed with the intended airflow. Sampling multiple 
sites within the chamber is needed to optimize and assure that 
the aerosol will be uniformly distributed. Raising or lower-
ing the airflow, or adding a small fan to better mix the air, 
may be required to optimize distribution of test aerosol. The 
sampler probe to be used during exposure should be located 
near the breathing zone of the animals, if after initial opti-
mization, regional intra-chamber concentration differences 
within the chamber remain that could skew the actual concen-
trations inhaled by the animals. The sampling system should 
be designed to transmit a representative air sample from the 
animal’s breathing zone to the sensor or collection medium 
without significant losses. The collection efficiency does not 
need to be 100%, but if sample losses cannot be avoided, at 
least the collection efficiency of the sampling system should 
be measured with the test substance and experimental condi-
tions to be used. In addition, care is required when the aerosol 
is likely to dynamically change due to coagulation, growth, or 
deposition in the test line. Above all, the investigator should 
strive for a constant collection efficiency.
For aerosols, the sampling system should be as straight and 
short as possible and designed to minimize impaction sites 
(i.e., bends, reducers, or turns) as well as diffusional losses of 
UFPs and ENPs. Rarely are aerosols monodisperse and, since 
most particle entrapment mechanisms are size-dependent, the 
collection characteristics of a given sampler will vary with 
aerodynamic and geometric particle size and sampler airflow. 
Aerosol collection efficiency will also change in response to 
sampler particle loading. A filter will be more efficient and 
trap finer particles more efficiently as dust collects on the 
surface, but the collection rate will gradually decrease as the 
pressure drop across the filter increases, thus reducing airflow 
unless a flow controller is used. In contrast, the efficiency of an 
electrostatic precipitator will drop as a resistive layer of par-
ticles accumulates on the collection electrode.
Sampling errors typically reflect the contribution of many 
small errors in the system rather than any single large source 
of error. Key items to be considered and evaluated in any such 
system include:
•	 sampling train leakage and losses
•	 variations in flow rate and sample volumes
•	 sampler aerosol or vapor collection efficiency
•	 sample stability in the collection media
•	 extraction efficiency from the sampling substrate
•	 analytical background or interferences/ artifacts 
introduced by the sampling substrate.
Additionally, the air sampling rate should be determined with 
a high degree of precision, and this can be performed with 
previously calibrated rotameters, gas meters, critical orifices, 
displacement monitors (dry or wet test meters), or electronic 
mass flow sensors. Much of this technology is described in 
classical texts on industrial hygiene.
For gases or vapors, sampling of the test atmosphere may 
be either instantaneous (i.e., grab sampling) or continuous 
in nature. If grab sampling is conducted, samples should be 
taken frequently enough such that fluctuations in concentra-
tion are detected. Samples may be collected in an evacuated 
glass or metal containers, inflatable flexible polymer bags, 
or by gastight syringes. Alternatively, gas or vapor samples 
may be taken from remote locations by directing the sample 
through nonreactive sample lines by using a vacuum pump 
and introducing aliquots of the sample stream directly into 
the analytical instrument. Continuous samples can be taken 
in this manner by pumping (or drawing) a constant stream of 
gas directly through a dedicated detector or by having the ana-
lytical device continuously cycle through sample streams from 
multiple exposure chambers.
Gases and vapors offer the least difficulty in sampling, since 
through the laws of diffusion, they typically mix and equili-
brate rapidly to achieve a uniform concentration within the 
chamber. Sampling typically involves the removal of the sub-
stance from the air by scrubbing through a solvent or reagent, 
adsorption to a collection surface, or collection in some type of 
container (e.g., an impermeable, nonreactive gas bag).
An extractive sampling system for gases, vapors, and aero-
sols includes sample lines from the breathing zone, a scrubbing 
device, an airflow or volume meter to determine sample vol-
ume, and a vacuum pump. Commonly, solvent-filled impingers

1616
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
are used for extractive sampling. Impingers consist of a sam-
pling tube extending through a reservoir of collecting media; 
the end of the tube at the bottom of the reservoir may be either 
open, narrowed, or fritted; the latter two modifications provide 
comparatively higher collection efficiency. The increased col-
lection efficiency is due to the smaller bubbles produced by the 
narrow or fritted impinger tips, which allows more complete 
diffusion of vapor from the bubbles into the surrounding sol-
vent. As a caveat, the solvent used in impingers can quickly 
evaporate, and, thus sampling with this method should not be 
continued for too long a time.
Solid adsorbent sampling devices are used for vapors and 
some gases. Solid adsorbent samplers consist of a small tube 
(about 100 mm length × 6 mm diameter) containing a sorbent, 
typically charcoal, which serves as the collection medium, 
and a vacuum source to draw a known rate of air over the 
sorbent. Charcoal is an effective sorbent for many compounds 
since its surface structure is microporous, thereby provid-
ing a high surface area for chemical adsorption. Importantly, 
charcoal has not only a large number of pores, but the pores 
exist in a range of dimensions to allow molecules of different 
sizes to enter them. Thus, charcoal can trap a wide variety of 
organic compounds. Other trapping agents include polymers, 
which can be engineered to trap certain chemicals, silica gel 
for inorganic substances, or molecular sieves. Once trapped, 
however, the sorbent must also allow the vapor/gas to be 
desorbed with high efficiency into a solvent for subsequent 
quantitation. Carbon disulfide is broadly used as a desorption 
solvent for charcoal tubes since it has good solvent proper-
ties and a low background with the flame ionization detector 
generally used in the analysis. Sampling is done at relatively 
low airflows to allow time for chemical adsorption yet must 
be of brief duration so as to not exceed the adsorbent capacity 
of the sorbent. Although it is relatively simple and inexpen-
sive, the use of gas sampling tubes in most toxicology studies 
is uncommon because of the labor involved in sample col-
lection and preparation relative to continuous, direct reading 
analyzers.
Gas sampling, using containment systems, is also relatively 
uncommon in toxicology studies. In this process, temporary 
storage vessels allow transport of gas or vapor samples from 
the exposure system to the analytical device. Examples include 
evacuated glass or stainless-steel containers or Tedlar® or 
Mylar® plastic bags. While some substances are stable in such 
containers, there are numerous logistical and practical limita-
tions to their routine use in inhalation toxicology studies. Gas 
sampling bags are physically cumbersome, require a logisti-
cal commitment to have a sufficient quantity of sample vessels 
available for the duration of testing and vessel transport to the 
analytical laboratory, require that chemical inertness and stor-
age stability be suitably demonstrated for the substance for the 
interval between sample collection and analysis, need to be 
decontaminated if vessels are reused for other substances, and 
may confound results from undetected leaks or sample losses 
during shipment.
Instruments for gas or vapor component identification and 
quantitation have brief response times (seconds or minutes). 
To make their determination, sensors in these instruments 
typically utilize either absorbance of infrared or UV radiation, 
flame ionization or photoionization, electron capture, or che-
miluminescence detection.
As with gas and vapor sampling, instantaneous (grab) or 
continuous (integrated) sampling techniques may also be used 
to collect particles. Instantaneous sampling involves removing 
a small volume of the atmosphere and separating the particle 
phase by either filtration or impacting the aerosol against a 
solid substrate on which the deposited particles are later char-
acterized (counted and sized and/or subjected to chemical 
analysis). For continuous sampling, a constant stream of the 
test atmosphere is sent to the analytical instrument. Analytical 
techniques that rely on physical forces such as gravity, impac-
tion, electrophoresis, thermophoresis, electrical mobility, iner-
tia, or diffusion techniques are used to collect particles in the 
instrument, with mass or optical properties subsequently used 
in their measurement. UFPs and ENPs require other metrics 
in addition to what may be a negligible mass concentration. 
These include number concentration, size distribution, and/
or particle surface measurements. Instrumentation commonly 
used for determining particle mass and size in inhalation stud-
ies is summarized in Table 30.3.
Grab sampling of aerosols usually involves either impac-
tors or filters. Fluid-filled impingers (as used for gases and 
vapors) have been used for aerosols, especially microbiologi-
cal aerosols, but since their collection efficiency is variable, 
they are not commonly used for nonviable particles. Instead, 
grab sampling for aerosols generally involves the use of fil-
ters for determining aerosol mass concentration in the cham-
ber. For non-hygroscopic aerosols, the accuracy of chamber 
aerosol concentrations is limited by the sensitivity of the 
volume measuring device as the weight of collected aerosol 
can be determined with great precision using modern analyti-
cal scales. Hygroscopic aerosols and filters may pick up (or 
lose) water vapor from/to the air and consequently can change 
weight during the interval between collection and filter weigh-
ing. To more accurately measure the weight of hygroscopic 
aerosols, standardized weighing techniques are needed such 
as desiccating (especially glass or cellulose acetate fiber filters) 
or equilibrating filters at a constant humidity prior to and dur-
ing weighing.
The choice of filter media available is broad and includes 
glass fiber and various polymer membranes (such as cellulose 
acetate, polycarbonate, polyvinyl chloride, polyvinylidene 
fluoride, or Teflon®). Polycarbonate membrane filters are per-
forated with holes of defined size. Unlike fiber, Teflon, and 
cellulose acetate filters that trap particles within the filter 
matrix, polycarbonate filters collect particles on the surface 
of the membrane. The choice of filter media is dictated by the 
need for high collection efficiency for particles of the size of 
interest, chemical inertness (to allow good recovery of the 
test substance for subsequent chemical analysis), and mini-
mal pressure drop across the filter (to allow high air sampling 
rates). Pressure drop is especially important for polycarbonate 
filters since smaller holes create a higher pressure drop, lim-
iting airflow through the filter. To some degree, smaller size 
holes in the filter membrane ensure a higher sample collection 
efficiency, but even larger pore filters collect many particles 
smaller than the pore size since some particles are deposited 
by interception and/or diffusion. Polycarbonate filters are

1617
Inhalation Toxicology
widely used for the analysis of mineral dust morphology by 
either optical or electron microscopy.
In addition to determining aerosol chamber concentrations 
via measurement of collected mass on filters, the filters may 
be extracted and analyzed to determine the content of the 
test substance. This is particularly valuable for mixed aero-
sol atmospheres where the content of each component must 
be evaluated, but this will require a specific analytical method 
for each component of interest. Clearly, in cases where test 
substances are to be extracted from the filter medium, the filter 
itself must be chemically unreactive with the test substance 
and should allow rapid and quantitative desorption of test 
substances for chemical analysis. Filters made from superfine 
glass fibers with diameters below 1 ōm are available for col-
lecting virtually all suspended particles; they typically have 
high particle loading levels and low airflow resistance. While 
glass fiber filters interfere negligibly with most subsequent 
chemical analyses, it should be noted that glass surfaces have 
imbedded hydroxyl groups that may bind metals or react with 
some substances. Regardless of composition, filter spiking and 
recovery studies should be evaluated before use to determine 
whether the particular filter substrate will interfere with the 
analysis.
As a complement to aerosol mass monitoring by filtration, 
real-time aerosol monitors can be used to provide analyti-
cal data for concentration adjustments. Light-based particle 
monitoring systems rely on light scattering through either 
refraction, reflection, or diffraction, all of which will vary 
with the particle’s optical size. The theory and explanation 
behind light-scattering devices are well established (Van de 
Hulst 1957, Kerker 1969, Bohren and Huffman 1998). While 
light-scattering techniques for measuring aerosol concentra-
tions provide instantaneous output and have found general 
acceptance for chamber monitoring, it should be noted that 
instrument response is a function of aerosol size, shape, 
color, concentration, and chemical composition. Thus, light-
scattering aerosol monitors need to be calibrated against 
other analytical methods with the specific aerosol of interest 
to allow quantitative use; even then, they are subject to coin-
cidence errors and are useful only at relatively low aerosol 
concentrations. These particle monitors do, however, display 
concentration changes immediately, and can usefully detect 
deviations in aerosol generator output or chamber airflow 
dynamics.
For particle sizing, cascade impactors are frequently used. 
Impactors are flow-through devices that withdraw aerosol 
from the test atmosphere. The impactor contains a series of 
impingement stages, each stage having progressively smaller 
nozzle dimensions. Since airflow through the impactor is con-
stant, each stage induces progressively increasing airstream 
velocities coupled with sequentially reduced impaction dis-
tances, yielding a separation of progressively smaller particles 
as aerosol makes its way through the unit. Particles depos-
ited at each stage can be weighed and/or examined micro-
scopically. The aerodynamic median size of an aerosol may 
be determined with the cascade impactor by calculating the

percentage of aerosol by weight on each stage using weight, 
radioactivity, or chemical analysis to determine the amount 
of material deposited on each stage. This process is shown in 
Figure 30.10. The same caveats with respect to the selection 
and the use of filter media and filter weighing techniques apply 
to cascade impactors. A major liability with cascade impactors 
is that impactor stages could potentially be overloaded with 
excessive particle mass. This may lead to the erroneous con-
clusion that aerodynamic sizes are smaller than they really are, 
due to particles bouncing off the target stage and subsequently 
captured on lower stages, giving the impression of finer par-
ticle size.
Instruments such as the TSI aerodynamic particle sizer can 
give direct, almost instantaneous readout of aerosol particle 
size. This device displays numbers of particles at their aerody-
namic diameters by measuring the particle velocity in a laser 
light-scattering sensing zone. Particles of different sizes are 
accelerated to different speeds when directed through a noz-
zle. The airstream is directed through dual-focus laser beams, 
one downstream from the other. The system’s circuitry mea-
sures the speed of the particle in an accelerating flow field, 
represented by the time taken to traverse the distance between 
the two lasers, and converts each time-of-flight measurement 
into an aerodynamic diameter. This device provides high-
resolution, real-time aerodynamic measurements of particles 
from 0.5 to 20 ōm, but processes particles at a concentration 
of about a few thousand particles/cm3; otherwise, coincidence 
errors occur; higher concentrations require dilution before 
measurement.
Unlike the TSI unit, the piezobalance cascade impac-
tor, known as the quartz crystal microbalance (QCM) by 
California Systems, measures mass concentrations (and aero-
dynamic diameters) directly. The QCM is a self-contained unit 
with a 6–10-stage cascade impactor that uses a quartz sens-
ing crystal in lieu of a filter on each stage. The crystal has an 
inherent oscillating frequency that changes in proportion to 
the mass of aerosol deposited on the crystal. This allows very 
small mass quantities to be detected and thus is ideal for very 
low (such as ambient aerosol) concentrations; size analysis of 
high concentrations can also be done with very short sampling 
durations to avoid stage overloading.
Based on the same measurement principle as the QCM, the 
tapered element oscillating microbalance (TEOM) has been 
widely used for aerosol mass measurements as described in 
Baron and Willeke (2001). The instrument provides continu-
ous and remote measurements of high sensitivity and can pro-
duce high-time-resolution data. The original TEOM has a 
recommended temperature setting of 50°C. There is evidence 
that semi-volatile components of aerosols are lost from the 
TEOM measurements at this temperature. The most likely 
explanation is that semi-volatile organic compounds, nitrates, 
and water are lost from the aerosols during sampling. In newer 
TEOM models, the operating temperature has been lowered to 
30°C to reduce losses of the semi-volatile components. New 
FIGURE 30.10  (a) Cascade impactor data from Table 23.3 plotted on arithmetic axes. (b) Size distribution data from (a), replotted using logarithmic 
scale for impactor 6cutpoint. (c) Size distribution data from (b), replotted using cumulative mass less than cutpoint diameter. (d) Size distribution data 
from (c), replotted using probability scale for cumulative mass. GSD is 2.2 ōm.

1619
Inhalation Toxicology
modifications have been made to dry the aerosol particles 
before measurement to reduce the adverse effect of ambient 
relative humidity.
Condensation Particle Counters (CPCs) count numbers 
of particles in the air. Particle number concentration is use-
ful, especially when dealing with nano-sized aerosols. 
Condensation nuclei can be detected after the condensation 
of water or other condensable vapor (often an alcohol such as 
butanol) from a supersaturated atmosphere onto the particle. 
The supersaturation in CPCs is typically quite large, around 
150%, allowing detection of particles as small as a few nano-
meters in diameter. Optical methods are usually used to detect 
the resulting droplets including photometric observation of 
light scattered by a collective of droplets in the optical sens-
ing volume. Bricard et al. (1976) improved the concept of this 
device by channeling the particles in a continuous flow with 
condensation of butanol to achieve supersaturation. Most cur-
rent CPCs employ single-particle counting and can detect con-
centrations up to 105 particles/cm3. Flow rates, lower detection 
diameter, and upper concentration ranges vary between coun-
ters; the particular counter used needs to be selected to suit the 
application.
The current state-of-the-art particle sizing instruments are 
based on the differential mobility particle sizer (DMPS; Liu 
and Pui 1974, Knutson and Whitby 1975, Kulmala et al. 2012) 
and the scanning mobility particle sizer (SMPS; Wang and 
Flagan 1990). These are basically the same type of instrument 
but have different electronic control mechanisms. They consist 
of a differential mobility analyzer (DMA), a CPC, and some 
electronic controlling devices, with everything controlled by a 
personal computer. The DMA enables the selection of only a 
very narrow size fraction of particles that then enters the CPC. 
The CPC counts the number of particles as usual, and since 
the size is already determined, information on both concentra-
tion and size is obtained. By changing the setting of the DMA, 
scanning over the whole particle size interval (3 nm to 1 ōm) 
is possible.
The aerosol time-of-flight mass spectrometry offers the 
real-time measurement capability to characterize aerosol par-
ticles by the aerodynamic size and chemical composition of 
individual aerosol particles sampled directly into the instru-
ment (Noble and Prather 1996). Such measurements are made 
in situ by combining a unique dual-laser aerodynamic particle 
sizing system to size and track individual particles through the 
instrument and laser desorption/ionization time-of-flight mass 
spectrometry to obtain correlated single-particle composition 
data. The size and chemical composition of 50–100 particles/
minute can be measured (up to 600/minute at high particle 
concentrations).
Samplers have been used for many years to electrostati-
cally deposit particles onto copper grids for transmission 
electron microscopy (TEM). A sampler is based on elec-
trostatic deposition Fierz et al. (2007) designed a sampler 
consisting of three functional units: an aerosol charger, a 
deposition zone, and an aerosol electrometer. The aerosol is 
drawn through the sampler at a flow rate of 0.5 L/minutes 
with an integrated pump. The charger is a unipolar diffusion 
charger. In the charging zone, the particles acquire a positive 
charge. Once charged, the aerosol enters the sampling region 
through a metallic inlet pipe. The TEM grid is mounted 
opposite to the aerosol inlet of the sampling region on a con-
ducting rod. Finally, the electrometer can be used to mea-
sure the charge of the collected particles on the TEM grid 
and thereby allowing an estimate of the number of deposited 
UFPs. The grid can then be viewed by TEM to determine the 
geometric particle size distribution and using the analytical 
features of the microscope such as energy-dispersive X-ray 
analysis, the chemical composition of inorganic particles can 
be determined. Reviews are available describing the impor-
tance of energy-dispersive X-ray analysis in the characteriza-
tion of inorganic particle exposures in pulmonary pathology 
(McDonald et al. 1999).
Another method to classify aerosol particles according to 
their mass-to-charge ratio and permits determination of par-
ticle density was reported by McMurry et al. (2002). This 
method works by balancing the electrostatic and centrifugal 
forces that act on particles introduced into a thin annular space 
formed between rotating cylindrical electrodes. Particles hav-
ing a mass-to-charge ratio lying in a certain narrow range are 
taken out continuously as an aerosol suspension. This tech-
nique involves the use of a DMA (Liu and Pui 1974, Knutson 
and Whitby 1975) to select particles of a given mobility size 
and an aerosol particle mass (APM) analyzer (Ehara et al. 
1996) to measure the mass of those particles. The APM classi-
fies particles according to mass, regardless of particle density 
or shape. By multiplying the number concentration down-
stream of the DMA by the particle mass, one can determine 
the mass concentration size dependently at a given DMA 
classifying voltage. The total mass concentration is obtained 
by integrating across the measured mass size distribution. 
Because measurements are made on particles suspended in the 
air, this technique avoids sampling artifacts associated with 
volatilization or adsorption that can occur with filter or impac-
tor sampling.
30.3.4  Exhaust/Scrubbing Systems
Exhaust systems are included in chamber design not only to 
assist in the flow of air through the exposure chambers but also 
to minimize discharge of test material into the environment. 
Federal, state, and local regulations on air emissions can be 
stringent in requiring that adequate abatement systems must 
be incorporated. Depending on the physical properties of the 
test material, liquid or solid absorbents (e.g., water for water-
soluble solvents and activated charcoal for low-level organics) 
may be employed to remove gases or vapors, whereas high-
efficiency particulate air filtering material may be appropriate 
for aerosols. Since liquid or solid absorbents become satu-
rated with test material, they must be monitored with suffi-
cient frequency to ensure that the adsorbent capacity has not 
been exceeded. In contrast, particle filters generally become 
more efficient as particle loading increases, but because air-
flow may be reduced concurrently when filters become loaded, 
the exposure chamber aerosol concentration may inadvertently 
increase. Depending upon the aerosol, thermal oxidation, 
incineration, and electrostatic precipitation are other means of 
treating chamber effluent and can be applied to scrubbing test 
material.

1620
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
30.4  Inhalation Toxicology Studies: 
Design Principles and Operation
Previous sections of this chapter have described each of the 
components needed for inhalation toxicology studies, and the 
multiple options available for these studies. In this section, prin-
ciples important in the design and operation of these systems, 
and how the key components integrate together, are discussed.
30.4.1  Considerations with In Vivo Exposures
The whole-body exposure mode is encountered frequently and 
allows the exposed test species to move freely in the atmo-
sphere containing the test substance. Whole-body inhala-
tion exposures will result in a portion of the substance being 
inhaled and deposited in the respiratory tract and a portion 
entering the body in other ways regardless of the test mate-
rial’s physical form. Gases or vapors can dissolve in the mucus 
fluid lining the respiratory tract and, via the mucociliary esca-
lator, reach the pharynx, where they are swallowed. Droplets 
or solid particles also reach the gastrointestinal tract via this 
mechanism. Contributions to the total dose can also occur by 
dermal (skin) absorption of the test substance. The normal 
grooming and preening activities of rodents both during and 
after inhalation exposures can also deliver the substance to 
the gastrointestinal tract. For example, rats were exposed to 
respirable zinc chromate dust either in conventional wire mesh 
cages or nose only in fiberglass tubes (so that the only expo-
sure was through the external nares). The caged rats excreted 
8.4 times as much fecal and 5.5 times as much urinary chro-
mium as rats in the tubes, indicating that a significant amount 
of dust could be ingested or absorbed following whole-body 
exposure (Langard and Nordhagen 1980).
Dermal absorption may contribute to the total body bur-
den from whole-body vapor exposures. The skin is a partial 
or incomplete barrier to inward or outward migration of most 
vapors. Because the surface area of the skin is large, a small 
flux of vapor across the entire skin surface can result in absorp-
tion of sufficient amounts of vapor through the skin to cause 
adverse health effects. McDougal et al. (1985) described a 
whole-body chamber in which rats were fitted with face masks 
and received positive-pressure, charcoal-filtered fresh air while 
being exposed to vapors of either dibromomethane (DBM) 
or bromochloromethane (BCM) (volatile organic chemicals 
for which their major metabolites could be easily measured 
in the blood). In rats exposed to the vapor by inhalation for 
4 hours, the percent absorption through the skin for DBM was 
5.8%, and for BCM was 4.2%. These uptakes were indepen-
dent of inhalation exposure concentration. Determination of 
the relative rates of absorption of vapor through the skin and 
the respiratory tract can be determined by comparing dermal 
absorption in rats without inhalation contact and inhalation 
by the usual methods. When inhalation is the route of expo-
sure, the rate-limiting process in absorption of substances with 
large blood–air partition coefficients, such as bromomethane, 
is the rate at which the vapor is transferred to the blood by 
alveolar ventilation (Andersen 1981). The rate-limiting pro-
cess of absorption for dermal exposure is a passage through 
the stratum cornea, which can be represented by the perme-
ability constant. Blood concentration measurements allow the 
input of both vapor inhalation and dermal vapor absorption 
integration when relating total delivered dose to biological 
effects (McDougal et al. 1986).
Because nose-only systems limit exposure of the test sub-
stances to the respiratory tract and not the entire body, the 
amount of test material needed is considerably reduced. 
This feature facilitates testing highly toxic and/or limited-
availability test substances. In addition, nose-only exposure 
units facilitate containment of test substance and thus sub-
sequent cleanup, and allow the chamber concentrations to be 
more easily and readily altered. Disadvantages of this system 
may include (1) loss of material to the fur of the head, (2) dif-
ficulty in obtaining a proper seal at the neck without impair-
ing circulation, (3) the possibility of animal stress induced by 
restraint and handling, and (4) the relatively limited number 
of animals that can be tested simultaneously. All of the previ-
ously mentioned disadvantages can be addressed in ways that 
make this type of testing useful in safety evaluation programs. 
To ensure that exposure conditions are uniform from animal 
to animal, a relatively large airflow is needed. For larger ani-
mals such as the dog and monkey, helmet or permanent trache-
ostomy exposures (mentioned previously) can be conducted. 
With helmet exposure, the fitting at the neck must be comfort-
able and easy to adjust. Designs including both inflatable col-
lars and thin rubber membranes have been used successfully.
A typical nose-only exposure unit employs an animal expo-
sure cylinder, generally constructed from rigid plastic (or glass). 
This tube is connected to an enclosure or exposure chamber 
that allows introduction and evacuation of test atmospheres 
and contains sampling probes to determine test concentrations, 
particle size distributions, temperature, and humidity. Rodent 
holders are usually made with plastic (polymethylmethacry-
late, polycarbonate), aluminum, or stainless-steel cylinders 
to conform to the general shape of the animals and are fitted 
with conical headpieces projecting into the plenum of the expo-
sure chamber. Some nose-only systems minimize rebreathing 
of test atmospheres by aiming for a constant flow of fresh test 
atmosphere directed onto the face of the animal while having a 
local exhaust near the nose, as shown previously in Figure 30.8. 
The animal holder needs to be of various sizes to accommodate 
rodents of differing sizes to minimize stress, provide careful 
support to minimize discomfort to the animal, and be ade-
quately ventilated to minimize heat retention within the tubes.
Both physical restraint and inadequate temperature control 
within the chamber can subject the test animals to additional 
stress. This seems to be especially true for inhalation devel-
opmental and/or reproductive studies. Pathological effects on 
the male reproductive system, independent of the inhaled test 
substance, have been reported in restrained male rats where 
inadequate temperature control occurred, leading to exces-
sive body temperature (Brock et al. 1996). Inhalation devel-
opmental toxicity studies conducted with restrained pregnant 
rodents have produced slight increases in maternal toxicity 
and either slight (Miller and Chernoff 1995) or no (Tyl et al. 
1994) increases in fetal anomalies when dams were restrained 
during the period of organogenesis. To minimize confinement-
induced stress in inhalation developmental toxicity studies in

1621
Inhalation Toxicology
mice, Dorman et al. (1996) utilized a rat restrainer tube to 
expose an unrestrained pregnant mouse, making essentially a 
whole-body exposure chamber for individual mice. Adaptation 
to the stress induced in rats and mice in nose-only inhalation 
tubes was examined. In naïve animals during the first hour 
of restraint, heart rate increased by 58 beats/minutes (BPM) 
(18.6%) in rats and by 174 BPM (32.3%) in mice, as compared 
to cage controls. Temperature increased by 2°C in mice and 
was unchanged in rats, compared to cage controls. Heart rate 
and temperature values remained within normal physiologic 
values during restraint. In rats, the response to restraint in 
nose-only holders was the same after 4 days regardless of 
whether the duration of restraint was increased gradually to 4 
hours/day or kept constant at 4 hours/day. In mice, the group 
that was gradually adapted had a statistically significantly 
higher heart rate and temperature after 4 days than the fixed-
duration adapted group. Rats and mice restrained for 4 hours/
day every day showed a gradual decrease in heart rate and 
temperature over time. Full adaptation to restraint required 
14 days of fixed-duration daily restraint (Narciso et al. 2003).
Overall, the choice of the nose-only exposure mode can 
affect the outcome of some inhalation toxicity studies, and its 
use requires that investigators include proper controls within 
their experimental design. Nonetheless, high-capacity nose-
only exposure systems, such as those described by Cannon 
et al. (1983), have been used for various acute and subchronic 
inhalation studies. A thorough description of the components 
used in this nose-only inhalation system, with details on auto-
mated collection methods for airflow regulation and test atmo-
sphere analysis, was presented by Pauluhn (1994).
Using a directed-flow nose-only exposure system at flow 
rates close to the minute ventilation of the animal, it has been 
possible to maintain desired concentrations. At an airflow rate 
of 2.5 times the animal’s minute ventilation, the concentration 
of test substance in inhaled air was reduced by less than 10% 
in one study (Moss et al. 2006).
Design considerations of head-only units are identical to 
those of nose-only units. Mask designs represent a unique 
kind of nose-only exposure and usually are limited to rela-
tively large animals. Masks for dogs (Bair et al. 1969), mon-
keys (Greenberg et al. 1977), ponies (Mauderly 1974), donkeys 
(Albert et al. 1974), chickens (Battista et al. 1973), and rats 
(Stavert et al. 1991) have been used successfully.
30.4.2  Chamber Selection and Operation
Most inhalation experiments utilize dynamic exposure condi-
tions. That is, the test atmosphere, comprising a test substance 
diluted with fresh air, is continually replenished. Thus, the test 
atmosphere flows through the exposure chamber and is then 
exhausted. Once the generation apparatus is turned on, the 
concentration in the chamber rises to a theoretical equilibrium 
value, which is the ratio of the flow of the substance to the total 
flow in the chamber. The equation describing the concentra-
tion–time curve is

Most frequently, the concentration–time relationship is 
described by t99, the time needed to attain 99% of the theo-
retical equilibrium concentration. For determination of t99, 
K = 4.605. At this time point, the concentration in the chamber 
may be considered constant, assuming no changes in air or 
test substance supply rates. The concentration–time charac-
teristics of a given chamber are described by the values of V 
and F, which are more descriptive than the use of air changes 
per hour (Silver 1946, MacFarland 1981). Air changes/hour is 
understood as the addition and withdrawal of a volume of air 
equal to the volume of the chamber. However, because added 
air always mixes with that already present, a complete change 
of air does not occur. Therefore, chamber airflow is more use-
fully described as t99.
The duration of exposure is defined as the interval from 
the start of the flow of the test substance to the point where 
delivery is discontinued. The exposure is terminated by stop-
ping the flow of substance to the atmosphere generator, which 
leads to the decline in the chamber concentration on an expo-
nential curve that is the inverse of the rising curve (Figure 
30.11). Animals are not removed from the chamber, nor are the 
chamber doors opened to observe the animals, until at least 
t99 minutes to allow sufficient time for the chamber contents 
to be fully eliminated. For longer term exposures, the rising 
and falling section of the curves can be neglected, and the 
exposure profile becomes a square wave form. For short expo-
sures, where exposure duration is less than 13 × t99, the system 
should include an airlock or some other protective mechanism 
(MacFarland 1976).
Chambers should be operated at a slight negative pressure 
(e.g., ′ 20 to 200 Pa) with respect to room pressure to pro-
tect personnel against leaks in the system. Chamber pressure 
should be monitored continually. As mentioned previously, 
homogeneous distribution of test substance within the cham-
ber should be established, and the actual concentration should 
be measured continuously or at least several times during 
the exposure. For exposures of 4–6 hours duration, a com-
mon practice is to sample every 0.5 hours (or at least hourly),

1622
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
although for regulatory purposes, much less frequent sam-
pling (i.e., 2–4 times per exposure) may be acceptable. The 
frequency of sampling should be dictated by the temporal 
stability of the test atmosphere generation and airflow supply 
systems. Continuous readout monitoring and control instru-
ments are especially useful in long-term studies, because they 
can prevent concentration excursions when appropriate alarms 
are integrated into the system. Some type of alarm instru-
mentation can prevent many weeks or months of effort from 
being ruined by sudden, unwanted elevations in the test sub-
stance concentration, which could be lethal or, at a minimum, 
severely compromise the experiment.
Inhalation chambers operated in a dynamic mode should 
have a reasonably uniform distribution of test substance to 
avoid differential animal exposures as mentioned previously. 
Due to their comparatively higher diffusion rates, gas and 
vapor concentrations show less intra-chamber variation than 
aerosols. Aerosols are also subject to size-dependent diffusion, 
impaction, and sedimentation losses, enhancing the challenge 
of maintaining uniform chamber concentrations. Furthermore, 
the uniformity of aerosol concentration may be reduced when 
animals are housed in the chamber since their body tempera-
tures lead to convection flow, which may cause concentration 
variations.
Control of the relative humidity, and even more importantly 
the internal temperature, of inhalation chambers is crucial to 
the integrity and interpretation of animal inhalation studies. 
As already noted, elevated temperatures can alter animal 
physiology, affect metabolism and ventilation, and increase 
the rate and type of chemical interactions. Ideally, chamber 
loading (the total volume of animals in a chamber) should not 
exceed 5% of the chamber volume to avoid heat-induced arti-
facts among test animals (Fraser et al. 1959). Coincidentally, 
chamber loading also affects the test substance equilibrium 
concentration in the exposure chamber. Silver (1946) reported, 
for example, that when the chamber loading exceeded 5%, 
excessive losses of test substance occurred, presumably by 
absorption to body surfaces. Temperature of the chamber 
should be monitored, ideally continuously but at least regu-
larly during the exposure. In some instances, such as when 
chamber loadings exceed this recommendation, chamber 
walls or inlet air may be cooled to maintain normal interior 
temperatures.
Heat-balance studies with rats in stainless-steel or glass 
chambers of equal size and using a room air intake of 
100 L/minutes showed that the chamber walls were effective 
at removing approximately 90% of the animal body heat as 
compared with the heat loss through the airstream. If conduct-
ing studies in chambers with low airflow rates, heat transfer 
to the surrounding environment can be increased by paint-
ing the chambers, by attaching cooling coils to the chamber 
walls, or by directing air-conditioning ducts directly onto the 
chamber Bernstein and Drew 1980). In addition, the proper 
control of relative humidity is essential for proper heat balance 
in rodents. Although it is generally recommended that relative 
humidity should be controlled within relatively narrow limits 
(e.g., 40–60%) to minimize adverse effects on feed consump-
tion and behavior on study outcome (Rao 1986), it has been 
reported that rats tolerated ranges of humidity from 3% to 80% 
in repeated-exposure studies without effects on body weights, 
food and water consumption, or respiratory tract histopathol-
ogy (Pauluhn and Mohr 1999).
30.4.3  Administered Dose
Dose is the amount of substance taken up by test subject(s), 
and it is a key concept in inhalation toxicology and a com-
mon focus of attention. Yet because it is challenging to mea-
sure individual respiratory parameters and the test substance’s 
physical/chemical properties (which both affect how inhaled 
materials deposit in the respiratory tract), determining an 
internalized dose by inhalation is much more complex than 
for other exposure routes. Of the total test material inhaled, 
a variable fraction may actually deposit, be adsorbed, and 
reach target tissues, which is described in detail in the next 
section of this chapter dealing with deposition and clearance 
of inhaled materials. This adsorbed fraction, in turn, depends 
on the local adsorptive, metabolic, and clearance processes 
that may modify the chemical composition and/or concentra-
tion of the inhaled substance where it lodges in the respira-
tory tract. Although these processes are not easily quantified, 
and so have made it harder for inhalation toxicologists to give 
FIGURE 30.11  Concentration–time relationship in a dynamic exposure chamber.

1623
Inhalation Toxicology
quantitative measures of inhaled dose, exposures with continu-
ous measurements of respiratory parameters, biomarkers, and 
mathematical modeling have improved, and so the field has 
progressed. Increasingly, sophisticated models can estimate 
toxicant concentrations in the blood and organs by treating the 
uptake, distribution, metabolism, and excretion of toxicants as 
a series of independent processes occurring at experimentally 
determined rates (Crapo et al. 1989).
Doses given in inhalation studies are most often charac-
terized as exposure conditions: concentration and duration 
expressed in conventional ways, e.g., “rats were exposed to x 
ppm (or x’ mg/m3) of substance y for 6 hours/day, 5 days/week 
for 13 weeks.” We summarize best practices for concentration 
expression as follows:
•	 Gases and vapors: volume (i.e., mole x/total moles, 
parts per million [ppm], or parts per billion [ppb]) or 
weight (milligrams per liter [mg/L] or milligrams per 
cubic meter [mg/m3]).
•	 Solid or liquid aerosols: weight (milligrams per liter 
[mg/L]).
•	 Fibrous materials or nano-sized UFP or ENP: num-
ber basis (number of fibers or particles per cubic cen-
timeter [#/cm3]).
•	 Fibrous aerosols: both respirable mass (mg/m3) and 
particle count (fibers/cm3).
Of note, with UFP or ENP aerosols and some low-density 
fibers, the number count may be very high but the mass con-
centration, in contrast, may be quite low.
For many inhaled substances, exposure concentration and 
time are interrelated. Haber (1924), for example, found that an 
animal subject’s response to a gas was related to the product 
of the concentration and the exposure time. According to this 
formula, known as Haber’s law, the product of the concentra-
tion (C) and exposure time (T) required to yield a physiologic 
or toxicologic effect is equal to a constant, C × T = K. As an 
easily observable endpoint, death is a common such effect, 
but there are others. Haber’s law has been used to predict the 
response to exposure conditions not yet studied—for example, 
predicting the 1-hour LC50 dose for a substance that has only 
4 hours of data.
During some short but finite time periods, a given endpoint 
might never be reached (i.e., death may not occur at concen-
trations that could be practically reached in short [e.g., 0.1, 1, 
or 10 minutes] time periods). Conversely, some substances’ 
exposure levels may not cause measurably adverse effects 
despite continuous, prolonged exposures. For these reasons, 
Haber’s law does not apply to all substances, but it does tend to 
apply when extrapolated to C × T values that differ by a factor 
of ~3–4 for a given C × T product. Rinehart and Hatch (1964) 
and Kelly et al. (1981) have shown, for example, that Haber’s 
law applied to phosgene gas and titanium tetrachloride aerosol 
when respiratory rate and lethality endpoints were respectively 
evaluated.
Gelzleichter et al. (1992) also showed that Haber’s law 
applied to nitrogen dioxide or ozone in rats exposed for 3 days 
over a fourfold range of exposure concentrations. However, for 
rats exposed to an ozone-nitrogen dioxide mix, lung damage 
was related to the peak concentration rather than the gases’ 
cumulative dose, which they attributed to the synergistic, 
adverse effects of the dual mixture. Tsuji et al. (2013) exposed 
SD and F344 rats to cigarette smoke at different concentra-
tions and duration, as a way to assess their respiratory study 
design. The authors found that, based on exposure biomarkers 
and biological responses, Haber’s law seemed to hold, i.e., the 
equivalent daily exposure dose C x T induced the pulmonary 
responses in the rats.
Shusterman et al. (2006) studied sensory irritation as the 
main endpoint, using exposures to a variety of chemicals and 
dusts, and found that varying the exposure concentration was 
proportionately more impactful than changing the exposure 
duration. With the exception of calcium oxide, their key test 
substances (chlorine, ammonia, formaldehyde, and 1-octene) 
did not follow Haber’s law. Hoyle et al. (2010) found the same 
thing for chlorine in an inhalational murine study.
ten Berge et al. (1986) quantitatively assessed how lethal-
ity was related (time and concentration parameters) to several 
irritant and systematically active substances. The authors 
found that for these substances, C × T did not always represent 
the response data over their exposure periods. Instead, Cn × T, 
where the exponent n ranges from 0.8 < n < 3.5, was found to 
often better predict mortality. Pauluhn et al. (1996) explain 
C × T relationships in acute inhalation testing done to classify 
hazards, in the setting of European Union regulations requir-
ing both 4-hour and 1-hour inhalation testing. Their analyses 
suggested that 1-hour testing did not add contributory value 
to the 4-hour exposure data for LC50 values, and they recom-
mended that where only 4-hour data were available, 4 should 
be a default value to convert 4-hour LC50 values to 1-hour 
LC50 values, independent of the test substance’s physical state.
The internalized dose is related to the atmospheric concen-
tration of the test substance, the duration of exposure, the indi-
vidual’s respiratory volume and frequency, and the deposition 
efficiency within the individual. These parameters are related 
according to the following general equation:
	
=

the respiratory tract

This equation assumes the aforementioned parameters are 
well-characterized for (1) the species and test substance 
under study, and (2) a given set of experimental conditions. 
In practice, such data are not generally available, especially 
for gases or vapors where doses have often been assessed in 
other ways. For a series of vapors, the internalized dose has 
been determined by measuring the net decrease in test sub-
stance concentration in the exhaled air vs. that in the inhaled

1624
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
air (Stott and McKenna 1984). Similar methodology has been 
used to measure disposition of ultrafine aerosols in the human 
respiratory tract. In a system designed to validate a rat-based 
physiologically based pharmacokinetic (PK) model of nasal 
extraction and metabolism of vinyl acetate (a water-soluble, 
reactive vapor), Hinderliter et al. (2005) exposed human vol-
unteers to radiolabeled vinyl acetate via nasal inhalation. 
Concentration measurements of vinyl acetate, and its metabo-
lite acetaldehyde, in nasal airways compared favorably with 
the model predictions, thus providing support for human risk 
assessments conducted for workplace exposures.
Other ways to estimate deposited dose include chemi-
cal assays for the total test substance or its metabolite in 
excreta, which is useful for radioactively labeled materials. 
Measurement of metabolite levels and DNA or protein adducts 
in biologic fluids or tissues represent other ways to assess sub-
stance exposure and internalized dose (Lucier et al. 1989, Dahl 
et al. 1991, Bond et al. 1992). DNA (Nicolette 2017) and protein 
adducts (Meyer and Bechtold 1996) occur when a segment of 
DNA or protein becomes covalently bound to a carcinogen or 
other toxin, thus damaging it and affecting its function. DNA 
adducts are advantageous in dose–response studies, because in 
contrast to the comparatively high toxic exposure concentra-
tions typical of cancer bioassay and tumor incidence studies, 
quantitative changes in metabolite DNA adducts can be mea-
sured at much lower exposure concentrations (Nicolette 2017). 
This method better estimates the shape of the dose–response 
curve at low concentrations, provided information is available 
to address the stability, extraction efficiency, and accuracy of 
the methods used to measure DNA adducts.
For dose estimates of radiation-induced health effects, the 
International Commission for Radiological Protection (ICRP) 
has written a large compendium on the human respiratory 
tract model. This document considers particle deposition of 
inhaled aerosols, subsequent biokinetics of radionuclides, and 
their effects on the organ of intake as well as target organs in 
the body (ICRP 1994, with updated supporting guidance ICRP 
2002).
The need to quantify internalized doses, particularly for 
human risk assessment, has encouraged research in the field 
of PK modeling (Andersen 1987). The use of PK or biokinetic 
modeling is finding wide acceptance among risk assessors and 
regulatory agencies as a way to predict the dose of a toxicant 
to a given tissue across species and under various exposure 
conditions. This approach describes the kinetic relationships 
between:
•	 physiologic factors (such as organ and body weights, 
respiratory rate, and blood flow)
•	 biochemical factors (such as substrate affinity for an 
enzyme and reaction velocity), and
•	 physicochemical factors (such as how much sub-
stance partitions into air, blood, or tissue) on the dis-
position of that substance within the body.
PK and biokinetic modeling is based on a thorough under-
standing of these processes in an experimental animal, and 
then extrapolating these parameters, with appropriate animal 
model validation, to predict what the target tissue doses and 
toxic effects will be in humans. Validated PK models, using 
accepted physiological values, may then be useful in pre-
dicting expected tissue levels of a substance (and thus organ 
toxicity) under various exposure regimens.
Indeed, PK models have successfully described the 
expected tissue doses of substances in rodents or humans for 
vapors such as butadiene (Himmelstein et al. 1997), benzene 
(Roy and Georgopoulos 1998), halogenated hydrocarbons 
(Vinegar et al. 1998), butoxyethanol (Lee et al. 1998), and 
diacetyl (Cichocki et al. 2014), as well as particulate matter 
such as powdered fire suppressants (Kimmell et al. 1998) or 
diesel soot, titanium dioxide (Stober et al. 1990), quartz dust 
(Stober 1999), formaldehyde (Kimbell 1994), vinyl acetate 
(Plowchalk et al. 1997), carbon tetrachloride (el-Masri et al. 
1996), and radionuclides contained in aerosol particles (ICRP 
1994). When PK modeling is employed, one must be appropri-
ately cautious with regard to how applicable the animal model 
is, and also with regard to the experimental techniques used to 
predict human values, so that the data will optimally help in 
human health risk assessment.
30.4.4  Other Exposure Modes for Respiratory Studies
This chapter has focused on inhalation methodology as the 
primary approach to inhalation toxicology and delivery of tox-
icants to the respiratory system. However, limited test materi-
als, the need for absolute control of administered dose, or other 
factors may dictate the use of intra-tracheal instillation meth-
odology or in vitro studies in which toxicants are delivered 
directly to the target cells of interest.
30.4.4.1  Intra-tracheal Instillation
The use of intra-tracheal instillation as an alternative to 
exposure of animals by inhalation deserves some comment. 
Sometimes the pulmonary effects of a substance cannot be 
easily evaluated by inhalation. Considerations including space, 
time, and/or economics, whether justifiable or not, may some-
times be used as the basis for decisions not to use inhalation 
as the route of test material exposure, but rather to use intra-
tracheal instillation techniques to get the material directly 
into the respiratory tract. The reasons for such a choice can 
also stem from insufficient quantities of test material or from 
safety concerns (e.g., extreme toxicity, flammability, explosion 
hazards). Using this method, the actual dose delivered to the 
lung of the experimental animal can be directly and precisely 
measured. This technique is inexpensive in that very small 
amounts of substance are needed; also, the needs for expensive 
chambers, generating apparatus, monitoring requirements, 
and support personnel are avoided. Also, because the tech-
nique is contained and uses relatively little material, exposure 
hazards to laboratory workers are greatly reduced compared to 
that of an inhalation study. Finally, materials that are not read-
ily respirable in rodents can be introduced to the lungs with 
this technique; for example, long fibers that can be inhaled by 
man, but not by rodents. The problem that limits the useful-
ness of intra-tracheal instillation as an exposure technique 
relative to inhalation is that the dose to the respiratory tissues 
can be variable and highly artificial and does not accurately

1625
Inhalation Toxicology
reflect the lung distribution of a substance with inhalation 
exposure. Additionally, intra-tracheal instillation techniques 
focus on the lower respiratory tract and, as such, cannot evalu-
ate responses that would occur in the nasopharyngeal region 
by inhalation exposure.
Intra-tracheal instillation involves a suspension of particles 
in a carrier liquid that is injected directly into the lumen of the 
trachea. By gravity, the fluid and particles flow into the areas 
of the lungs in a manner that cannot be entirely controlled. 
The carrier liquid is then rapidly absorbed into the pulmonary 
circulation, leaving the particles on the internal surfaces of the 
lung. This technique permits the introduction of a wide range 
of doses and substances to the lung in a short time period. In 
larger animals, localized exposures to specific areas or lobes 
of the lung are possible, often allowing the contralateral lung 
to serve as a control (for non-systemically acting agents). This 
technique was first applied by Kimura (1923), using rabbits 
and guinea pigs to study the response to various coal tars. 
Early works have described in detail useful methodologies 
found to be useful in the mouse (Nettesheim and Hammons 
1971, Kouri et al. 1976), in the rat (Blair 1974), and in the ham-
ster (Saffiotti et al. 1968, Brain et al. 1976). The procedures 
used in these studies are quite similar, differing mainly in the 
maximum amount of total liquid used to deliver the test sub-
stance to the lung without harming the animal.
In small rodents, intra-tracheal instillation is done by insert-
ing a catheter or needle trans-orally through the mouth and 
epiglottis into the tracheal lumen. In larger species (includ-
ing human), a fiber-optic bronchoscope can be used to more 
precisely visualize the instillation site. Because the animal 
must not move during the procedure, the choice of anesthetic 
is important, with short-acting materials that suppress reflexes 
for a minimal period of time being preferred. Physiologic 
saline is the vehicle most frequently used to suspend or solubi-
lize the test substance, and dosage volumes are adjusted for the 
body weight of the animal. Control with vehicle alone needs to 
be part of the experimental design since the vehicle may evoke 
a mild transient inflammatory response. Surfactants can be 
used to improve the suspension properties of dusts, but the sur-
factant effect(s) on the lungs also needs to be evaluated. The 
addition of surfactants is especially needed in order to prevent 
clumping of organic materials in an aqueous vehicle. Sager 
et al. (2013) developed a saline dispersion medium containing 
D-glucose, species-specific serum albumen, and 1,2-dipalmi-
toyl-sn-glycero-3-phosphochline (a major component of lung 
surfactant) which was used to disperse multi-walled carbon 
nano-tubules. Recently, this dispersion medium was used in 
a study comparing the response to secondary particles formed 
from vehicle exhaust from cars using different ethanol-blended 
gasoline mixtures (da Silva et al. 2019).
Although intra-tracheal instillation enables administration 
of large amounts and even non-respirable sizes of particu-
late matter that would otherwise not be able to gain access to 
the lung, the pattern of deposition is localized, non-uniform, 
dependent on gravity, and very unlike those resulting from 
inhalation (Brain et al. 1976). These investigators studied 
the pulmonary distribution of particles labeled with 99Tc in 
both rats and hamsters with either intra-tracheal instillation 
or aerosol inhalation. Particle distribution patterns in the lung 
following inhalation were distributed evenly within a given 
lobe lung with greater amounts deposited in the apical lobes. 
Instillation had a predominantly central pattern with an uneven 
distribution. Others (Brody and Roe 1983, Pritchard et al. 
1985) also found variability in comparing the two methods 
using cerium oxide particles in rats as did Dorries and Valberg 
(1992) using ferric oxide particles. In all these studies, depo-
sition was greater with instillation than with inhalation, and 
there was little penetration of instilled particles to the periph-
eral lung. Uneven and patchy distributions were confirmed 
after intra-tracheal instillation of suspensions containing 
either submicron titanium dioxide (TiO2) particles or nano-
sized primary TiO2 particles in comparison to intra-tracheal 
inhalation (Osier et al. 1997). In contrast, the distribution of 
both short and long glass fibers in rats was reportedly similar 
using either inhalation or instillation (Henderson et al. 1995). 
With intra-tracheal instillation, it is important to coordinate 
and optimize the timing of the instillation with the animal’s 
inspiration, because if inadequately timed (for example, during 
expiration), the instilled volume may be expired completely.
Intra-tracheal aspiration was developed as a derivative of 
intra-tracheal instillation. In this procedure, a droplet of par-
ticle suspension is placed onto the glottis while the nose of 
the animal was closed. When opening the nostrils, the droplet 
is accelerated and partially dispersed into the trachea during 
a high flow, gasp-like breath of the animal. Aspiration was 
compared to inhalation and intra-tracheal instillation using 
99mTc radiolabeled nano-sized sulfur colloids followed by 
gamma camera imaging using a pinhole collimator (Foster 
et al. 2001). The authors observed higher particle doses and 
higher 24 hours retention in the lungs after instillation and 
aspiration compared to inhalation. Only after inhalation was 
the particle distribution in the lungs rather homogeneous. 
Yet they showed that the aspirated suspension volume criti-
cally determines the deposited dose and retention. Also, cyto-
metric analysis of the bronchoalveolar lavage fluid showed 
increased numbers of eosinophil and neutrophil granulocytes 
suggesting an inflammatory response of the peripheral lungs 
to contaminants on the glottis, which were flushed with the 
droplet during the high airflow inspiration. Similarly, a higher 
delivered dose was reported for SWCNTs, but a less even and 
patchy distribution was observed when compared to inhala-
tion of aerosolized small agglomerates of SWCNT (Shvedova 
et al. 2008). While the results seem to be promising in mice, 
highly variable deposits of radiolabeled NPs were found on the 
thoracic airway epithelium. Mucociliary cleared particle frac-
tions varied from 15% to as much as 80%, as determined by 
gamma camera imaging during the next 24 hours. Hence, this 
highly variable dose to the airways results in an inverse, but 
also highly variable dose to the lung periphery, which hampers 
the advantage of aspiration in rats considerably.
Another aerosol delivery method utilizes a pressurized air 
jet to nebulize particle suspension directly through a tiny noz-
zle within the trachea. Using 2 ōm monodisperse 111In radio-
labeled polystyrene particles were dispersed through a nozzle 
at the end of a bronchoscope placed two-thirds down into the 
trachea of beagle dogs (Kreyling and Wohland 2007). In all 
cases, the particle bolus deposited at the carina and first main 
bronchus where more than 95% of the administered particles

1626
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
were found. Similar commercial devices have been available. 
The Penn-Century company developed a microsprayer as well 
as a dry powder insufflator that delivered liquid and non-liquid 
aerosols respectively from the tip of a tube inserted into the 
airways with distribution patterns significantly better than 
intra-tracheal instillation. Unfortunately, this company is no 
longer in business, but a number of laboratories have reported 
successful use of this device (Diemel et al. 200; da Silva et al. 
2019).
30.4.4.2  In Vitro Toxicity Test Methods
Within the last three or so decades, the focus of toxicology has 
trended from whole-animal toxicity studies toward alternative 
in vitro toxicity methods; this has come from authorities and 
regulatory bodies deciding to reduce and even ban the use of 
animals (Silbergeld 1998, Gad 2000, Bakand et al. 2005, EC 
2013, Grimm 2019). In vitro toxicology is the study of isolated 
organs, tissues, and cell cultures to assess the toxic effects of 
substances (Hayes and Markovic 1999). Not surprisingly, the 
growth of in vitro toxicity test methods has been driven by 
varied scientific and socioeconomic factors, including but not 
limited to the trend of authorities and regulatory bodies decid-
ing to reduce and even ban the use of animals (Silbergeld 1998, 
Gad 2000, Bakand et al. 2005, EC 2013, Grimm 2019).
The ethical/technical bases for continuing the use of ani-
mals in research require that toxicologists address several fac-
tors: (1) reducing how many animals are needed, (2) refining 
test protocols to minimize suffering, and (3) replacing cur-
rent animal tests with good in vitro methods that give valid, 
toxicological data (Verheugen 2006). The need to assess the 
potential toxic effects of many substances and formulations 
has influenced the search for rapid, sensitive, and specific test 
methods. Currently, in vitro methods cover a broad range of 
techniques and models, and a standardized battery of in vitro 
tests, if validated, can assess acute, local, and systemic toxicity.
A practical strategy for in vitro concepts and approaches in 
inhalation toxicity has been proposed by the European Centre 
for the Validation of Alternative Methods (ECVAM) (EURL 
ECVAM 2013). Using existing literature, their strategy looks 
at the physicochemical characteristics of test substances and 
predicts possible toxic effects based on structure–activity 
relationships. Physicochemical characteristics include enti-
ties such as molecular structure, solubility, vapor pressure, 
pH sensitivity, electrophilicity, and chemical reactivity; these 
key properties often yield critical information which allows 
hazards to be categorized, and toxicities to be predicted (Gad 
2000, Faustman and Omenn 2001).
Of note, a large array of human- and animal-derived cell 
lines are available for in vitro culture and propagation that can 
serve as models of the respiratory tract. In addition to species 
specificity, male or female origin can also be included in such 
model systems. Specific cell lines, available from sources such 
as American Type Culture Collection (www.atcc.org), may be 
used in various in vitro toxicology assays and model different 
parts of the respiratory tract, with attendant useful impact in 
the assessment of normal cell function, various inflammatory 
states, and carcinogenesis. Many cells available for use in toxi-
cology studies are immortalized; others might subsequently be 
able to be transfected. Table 30.4 lists some of the frequently
