used cell lines modeling the different anatomic parts of the 
respiratory tract, along with references showing examples of 
how they have been applied beneficially within in vitro toxi-
cology settings. In addition, the use of multiple cell types in 
the same culture is common and often provides useful infor-
mation, especially with regard to the differences in responses 
between the cell types, or the many ways interactions between 
them can facilitate mechanistic understanding (Shi et al. 2016, 
Mandarino et al. 2020).
In vitro exposure techniques are varied, and include direct 
exposure to the test substance itself, exposure to air samples 
containing the substance(s) of interest, submerged exposure 
conditions, intermittent exposure, or continuous direct expo-
sure. The last two items apply to gaseous tests.
In vitro screening tests may identify cellular responses that 
allow the test substance’s toxicity to be estimated. Based on 
the result, in vitro tests may be followed by a second phase 
using isolated cells specific to the target organs, for example, 
nasal olfactory cells, fibroblasts, or mesothelial cells (Lambre 
et al. 1996). Freshly isolated cells in suitable culture media that 
can mimic biotransformation activities and cellular functions 
comparable to the in vivo environment are preferable to long-
term cultures or cell lines that may differentiate, lose their 
organ-specific functions, and lack the enzyme systems for bio-
transformation (Barile 1994, Lambre et al. 1996).
The effects of air contaminants have been studied using 
several in vitro exposure techniques. Most have used studies 
of isolated particulate substances added to a culture medium 
(Nadeau et al. 1995, Becker et al. 2002). Although such expo-
sure conditions may suffice for soluble test materials, this may 
not simulate in vivo dose and dose rate exposure from airborne 
aerosols. Moreover, such techniques may often ignore particle 
size effects, which is a crucial issue for in vivo testing. Past 
in vitro research has looked at the effects of suspended and 
extracted particles from atmospheric aerosols (Hamers et al. 
2000, Glowala et al. 2002) and cigarette smoke condensate 
(McKarns et al. 2000, Putnam et al. 2002).
The toxic effects of sulfuric acid aerosols on human airway 
epithelial cells (Chen et al. 1993) and the effects of ozone on 
primate bronchial epithelial cells (Jabbour et al. 1998) and 
human bronchial cells (Mogel et al. 1998) have been stud-
ied using cells grown on collagen-coated membranes. Toxic 
effects of individual airborne substances such as ozone and 
nitrogen dioxide, and complex mixtures such as diesel motor 
exhaust (Knebel et al. 2002) and cigarette smoke (Aufderheide 
et al. 2003), were studied using cultured human lung cells on 
porous membranes permeable to culture media.
Direct exposure using an ALI gives a reproducible contact 
between chemically and physically unmodified airborne con-
taminants and target cells, and so may reflect more closely the 
in vivo inhalation exposure (Knebel et al. 2002, Aufderheide 
et al. 2003, Paur et al. 2011, Lacroix et al. 2018, Upadhyay and 
Palmberg 2018). Submerged cell systems, in contrast, when 
used to study the effects of deposited aerosol particles on respi-
ratory tract epithelia, may be suboptimal. This is because (1) 
the cells may have had functional changes under submersion, 
and (2) the submersed particles may have had physicochemical 
changes in the cell media, such that overall, results from sub-
mersed exposure systems may be invalid and not comparable 
to the in vivo exposure (Paur et al. 2011). Hence, test systems 
with an ALI of epithelial cells often have better physiological 
conditions, and results are likely to provide better information. 
Much greater detail on these systems is provided in the rel-
evant section of this chapter (ALI Cell Culture Systems), to 
which the reader is referred.
A key principle is that everything can be toxic if the expo-
sure amount is big enough (Timbrell 1998). In this regard, we 
quote the 16th-century Swiss physician Paracelsus: “All things 
are poison and nothing is without poison, only the dose per-
mits something not to be poisonous.” This implies that sub-
stances with a low chemical hazard have a low safety risk. But 
if there is a high enough exposure to these low-hazard sub-
stances, then of course even they can be harmful or fatal. And 
of course, the opposite can also be true: high chemical hazard 
but low exposure may yield a low safety risk.
Using the most relevant dose metric for particle toxic-
ity is very important (Schmid et al. 2009, Grass et al. 2010). 
While the common dose metric for micron-sized particles is 
their mass, this becomes much harder for UFP or ENP. This 
is because, for such small particles, the various metrics may 
diverge widely: aggregate mass may be small, but particle 
number and aggregate surface area might be large, for exam-
ple. Hence, a single parameter (e.g., number, mass, or surface 
area) is unlikely to adequately convey the toxic effects of most 
NPs, even if it suffices as a dose metric in other settings. While 
particle mass is likely to be a reasonable dose metric for solu-
ble or biodegradable NPs, non-biodegradable or partially inert 
NPs reside for a long time in tissue. During this time, such NPs 
can interact with the biological environment via the surface–
tissue interface. This is consistent with mounting evidence 
that surface area, rather than mass or number, best predicts the 
toxic response to numerous types of NPs (Oberdorster et al. 
2007, Stoeger et al. 2007, Donaldson et al. 2008, Stoeger et al. 
2009, Hadrup et al. 2020).
Though surface area may be a highly relevant dosimetry 
metric for insoluble, non-biodegradable particles, others also 
must be considered. For example, frustrated phagocytosis, 
where macrophages become overwhelmed by the size and/or 
physicochemical characteristics of the material to be ingested, 
is encountered for rigid, insoluble fibers >10 ōm (e.g., asbestos 
and CNTs) in the lungs (Poland et al. 2008). The principles 
of fiber toxicology and Stanton’s hypothesis are discussed 
more deeply elsewhere in this chapter and in the literature 
(Bernstein et al. 2013, Boulanger et al. 2014, Sato et al. 2020). 
Also, a more detailed discussion of the effect of the physico-
chemical properties of NPs on the dose definition has been 
presented by Grass et al. (2010). Wills et al. (2017) studied 
agglomeration states in NP in vitro assays, which are influ-
enced by physiological and experimental conditions, and how 
such states affect the dose that assay cells effectively receive.
For submerged in vitro cell exposure systems with standard 
media heights of >1 mm above the cell layer, the exposure–
dose relationship is unfortunately complex. Diffusion, sedi-
mentation, and thermal convection produce different transport 
rates between particles of varied sizes and densities in differ-
ent test cavities, and between materials with varying agglom-
eration states. How many particles reach the cells in a given 
time period (cellular dose) will thus be very different, and

1628
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
there is no easy relationship between particle characteristics 
and the amount reaching the cells to give us a ready solution.
For example, despite concluding that nominal media con-
centration (NMC) (i.e., the theoretical concentration when 
a test solution is prepared) was an appropriate dose metric 
in vitro, Lison et al. (2008) showed no clear relationship 
between NMC and cellular dose for 35 nm amorphous silica. 
More important, perhaps, is that media concentrations may 
misrepresent the dose to the cell by many orders of magni-
tude when particle size is considered (Limbach et al. 2005, 
Teeguarden et al. 2007). This is based on century-old princi-
ples of particle motion in liquids (Einstein 1905, Dusenberry 
2009).
Based on Mason and Weaver’s (1924) theoretical work 
on how particles diffuse and settle in fluids, Hinderliter and 
coworkers (2010) developed an in vitro sedimentation, dif-
fusion, and dosimetry model for how NPs transport in cell 
culture media. While this model had to estimate the gravi-
tational density of the NPs that may agglomerate or absorb 
proteins onto their surfaces, Cohen et al. (2014) developed a 
rather simple method to measure the apparent density of the 
tested NPs in the culture medium which improved the pre-
diction of NP transport in the culture medium considerably. 
Based on this model, Figure 30.12 shows the transport time 
(minutes) of solid, non-agglomerated gold nanospheres for 
a 1 mm distance. Surprisingly, this issue has received little 
attention in the literature, where the focus seems to be pre-
dominantly on the suitability of assays, cell types, and meth-
ods of characterization (Doak et al. 2009, Donaldson 2009, 
Kroll et al. 2009, Marquis et al. 2009, Park et al. 2009, Stone 
et al. 2009).
In this regard, dose levels can be more helpfully expressed 
as NPs per cell. For a number concentration of 3 × 104/cm3 
in ambient air, where the majority (80–90%) can be consid-
ered NPs (mobility diameter < 100 nm [Kreyling et al. 2003]), 
~2.3 × 1011 NPs deposit onto the alveolar epithelium daily. 
Assuming the alveolar epithelium has 2 × 1010 epithelial type 
I cells, 3 × 1010 epithelial type II cells, and 6 × 109 alveolar 
macrophages (Stone et al. 1992), this implies that ~10 NPs/
cell would deposit daily, presuming that deposition density 
per alveolar surface area is uniform (not unreasonable, since 
NPs behave as per their diffusional properties). This rather low 
value is important when considering doses of tens of ōg per 
one million incubated cells—which is several orders of magni-
tude higher than a realistic daily dose of inhaled and deposited 
NPs.
The virtual cell-based assay (VCBA) is a recent develop-
ment that has the potential to improve testing strategies in 
toxicology, including nanomaterials (Graepel et al. 2017). For 
an in vitro system, the VCBA uses differential equations to 
estimate the time-dependent concentration of a toxicant in the 
cell culture. Physicochemical parameters and characteristics 
of the cell line influence the calculations of the various con-
centrations of the toxicant in cell compartments. The model 
can be used for both in vivo extrapolation, and improved 
design of in vitro testing. As the VCBA is developed for more 
cell lines and cell culture methods, it could benefit those sys-
tems most commonly used in inhalational toxicology. There 
is great potential for this method to also positively impact 
nanomaterials toxicology; Graepel et al. (2017) review pro-
cesses that would need to be implemented in the VCBA for 
this to be optimized.
A critically important area of in vitro toxicology is toxi-
cogenomics or the study of the interaction between an indi-
vidual’s genetic makeup and their biochemical and physiologic 
responses to toxicant exposures. A separate section elsewhere 
in this chapter is dedicated to this topic.
30.4.4.3  ALI of Cell Culture Systems
ALI cell culture systems are an in vitro model of the human 
respiratory system, done in a physiologically relevant way, 
and which don’t require in vivo animal studies. They are 
regarded, in terms of relevancy, as intermediate between clas-
sic in vitro models and animal models (Lacroix et al. 2018). 
Successful past applications include toxicology studies (Cao 
et al. 2018) as well as transcriptome analyses, pathogens, 
and studies of respiratory diseases such as asthma and cystic 
fibrosis (Krunkosky et al. 2007, Dvorak et al. 2011, Ostrowski 
et al. 2012, Comer et al. 2013). In ALI systems, primary 
human bronchial epithelial cells are cultured at an interface 
commonly delineated by a permeable membrane insert, with 
culture medium initially on both sides, then once conflu-
ence is reached, only in the bottom (basal) chamber, leaving 
air on top (apical side) (Stemcell Technologies, Inc., 2020). 
Additional reviews are available such as those by Lacroix 
et al. (2018) and Upadhyay and Palmberg (2018). Thus, the 
anatomic configuration of the human airway is modeled, with 
exposure to air being important in making the system physi-
ologically relevant. Early versions of modern ALI cell culture 
techniques were introduced in the 1970s (Voisin et al. 1975, 
1977a, b).
FIGURE 30.12  Impact of particle diameter on the transport in in vitro 
cell culture systems. The theoretical time to transport non-agglomerating 
gold nanospheres of known density over a distance of 1mm is plotted 
against particle diameter, demonstrating a clear size-dependency of dif-
fusion and sedimentation. NPs ≥40 nm in diameter are primarily driven 
by diffusion, while NPs >40 nm are primarily driven by sedimentation. 
(Cohen and Demokritou (2014) with permission.)

1629
Inhalation Toxicology
The essential aspects of in vitro exposure systems such as 
ALI cell cultures, to simulate realistic inhalation exposure 
conditions, are:
•	 complex pulmonary cell systems, which can be culti-
vated for at least several hours at the ALI
•	 direct contact between cultivated cells and inhalable 
substances without interfering medium
•	 uniform exposure of the entire cell layer
•	 temperature and humidity conditions that maintain 
cell integrity (T ~ 37°C; RH > 85%)
•	 precise control of the pollutant levels (Rasmussen 
1984, Aufderheide 2005, 2008) for accurate 
dosimetry
A variety of such ALI cell exposure systems have been 
described (Massey et al. 1998, Aufderheide and Mohr 1999, 
2000, Fukano et al. 2004, Aufderheide 2005, Bitterle et al. 
2006, Mulhopt et al. 2008, BeruBe et al. 2009). While most 
rely on diffusion and/or gravitational settling as deposition 
mechanisms, some exposure systems have been introduced 
which use electrostatic deposition for bipolar- or unipolar-
charged particles (Savi et al. 2008, Stevens et al. 2008).
Most such investigations are done with monocultures; how-
ever, studies have shown that cocultures—a combination of 
different cell types—influence the observed cellular response 
(Lehmann et al. 2009). A triple cell co-organ culture in vitro 
model of the human airway wall has been developed to study 
the cellular interplay and the response of epithelial cells, 
human blood monocyte–derived macrophages, and dendritic 
cells to particles (Rothen-Rutishauser et al. 2005, 2008, Blank 
et al. 2007, Rothen-Rutishauser 2007). In this model, mono-
layers of two different epithelial cell lines, A549 (Lieber et al. 
1976) and 16HBE14o epithelia (Forbes 2000), were grown 
on a microporous membrane in a two-chamber system. After 
human blood–derived monocytes were isolated and differ-
entiated into macrophages and dendritic cells (see also para-
graph primary cell cultures), they were added at the apical and 
basal sides of the epithelium, respectively. After the triple cell 
coculture was established, cell densities of macrophages and 
dendritic cells within the culture were respectively quantified 
using the specific surface markers CD14 and CD86. The quan-
titative occurrence of macrophages and dendritic cells was 
found to resemble the in vivo situation very closely (Blank et al. 
2007). After this thorough evaluation, the model was exposed 
to particles (either airborne or suspended in a medium) of dif-
ferent materials (polystyrene, titanium dioxide, gold) and of 
different sizes (≥1 ōm) (Blank et al. 2007, Rothen-Rutishauser 
2007, Rothen-Rutishauser et al. 2007, 2008, Brandenberger 
et al. 2010). How the particles translocated and localized in 
cells was studied, as well as how the particles affected cellu-
lar interplay and signaling. The findings showed that dendritic 
cells and macrophages collaborate as sentinels against fine 
polystyrene particles (~1 ōm diameter) by building a transepi-
thelial interdigitating network of cell processes (Blank et al. 
2007). The translocation of NPs into the different cell types, 
however, was different compared to their larger particle coun-
terparts (Rothen-Rutishauser et al. 2007).
From such studies, we can see that, besides their good 
physiologic relevance and opportunities for cocultures, air–
liquid exposure systems in toxicology have a great advantage 
in allowing high dosing of respiratory epithelial cells. This 
applies to protocols where such would be difficult or impos-
sible in a standard inhalation study, because of the aerosol 
physics of NP deposition.
Developments in ALI research have focused on organ-on-a-
chip approaches for lung tissue (Huh et al. 2010), in-depth stud-
ies of how conventional and ALI culture systems differ with 
regard to NPs and biomarker evaluation (Lenz et al. 2013, Loret 
et al. 2016), and culture systems that simulate the pressure dif-
ferences under breathing movement conditions (Schurch et al. 
2014). Other recent studies have used ALI systems containing 
human bronchial epithelial cells to successfully study expo-
sures to dihydroxyacetone (Wang et al. 2019), polycyclic aro-
matic hydrocarbons (Chang et al. 2019), animal dung biomass 
fuel smoke (McCarthy et al. 2016), and cadmium (Xiong et al. 
2019). Bayeler et al. (2018) used ALI cultures of human bron-
chial epithelial cells from normal and chronic obstructive pul-
monary disease patients to evaluate the effects of MWCNTs. 
This study used these ALI models successfully, by showing 
responses to positive control toxicants while not showing sub-
stantial adverse effects from acute exposure to the nanotubes. 
Rossner et al. (2019) recently evaluated the gene expression 
effects of auto emissions on ALI systems consisting of human 
bronchial epithelial cells, and a three-dimensional model of the 
human airway consisting of basal, goblet, and ciliated cells. 
Their study was significant in demonstrating that the three-
dimensional cellular system was suitable for this type of work.
Regardless of the techniques used, before in vitro methods 
can be formally done in place of traditional in vivo inhalation 
protocols to classify hazards and/or assess risks, validation 
efforts must ensure that the approaches, results, and conclu-
sions reached from such techniques apply to, and are consistent 
with, the objectives of international testing guidelines. Recently 
an international workshop was convened to address this issue, 
and the reader is referred elsewhere for an in-depth discussion 
(Lacroix et al. 2018), which discusses the principles of valida-
tion and how that process would ideally look for ALI systems. 
Challenges outlined at the meeting included deciding what 
should be validated among the large numbers of models and 
endpoints, lack of consensus on the most pressing research ques-
tions which could inform validation, and no widely accepted 
standard for benchmarking methods, especially as they relate to 
human relevance vs. animal data (Lacroix et al. 2018).
Consequently, for in vitro studies, exposure and dose metrics 
must be clearly distinguished as they pertain to nanomateri-
als. NMCs (ōg/mL, SA/mL, #/mL) and their derivatives most 
commonly used in in vitro studies do not measure dose; they 
measure exposure, and so do not accurately reflect the amount 
of material accessing the cells. Processes such as diffusion, 
sedimentation, and perhaps convection, all of which depend 
on NPs’ size, shape, and density, influence the relationship 
between the NPs in the solution above cells and NPs accessing 
the cells (Limbach et al. 2005, Teeguarden et al. 2007), the 
latter being a measure of NP dose. The general tendency to use 
exposure rather than dose metrics is thus misplaced since the 
two entities don’t have a direct, consistent relationship.

1630
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
30.5  Deposition, Clearance, and 
Retention of Inhaled Substances
30.5.1  Gases and Vapors
Gases and vapors can both be inspired under ambient test-
ing conditions. Gases, by definition, exist in the gaseous 
state at standard temperature and pressure, while vapors 
represent the gas-phase components from substances that 
are solid or liquid at standard temperature and pressure. As 
the temperature rises, gas-phase components (represented by 
their vapor pressure) from a liquid will increase. Thus, liq-
uids with higher vapor pressure will have more, i.e., a higher 
concentration of molecules in the gas phase, relative to those 
with lower vapor pressure. For the inhalation toxicologist, 
liquid- or solid-phase test substances are especially challeng-
ing since test atmospheres may exist in both gas and solid/
liquid forms, necessitating consideration of their different 
depositional, clearance, analytical, and biological response 
characteristics.
Inhaled gases and vapors can be absorbed throughout the 
respiratory tract causing either local injury or systemic tox-
icity. Absorption depends largely on the physiochemical 
characteristics (e.g., concentration, water solubility, partition 
coefficient) of the inhaled substance and physiologic charac-
teristics of the respiratory tract (e.g., airflow, respiratory rate, 
tissue perfusion, local metabolism).
The net force driving the absorption of inhaled gases or 
vapors is diffusion, the net movement of molecules down a 
concentration gradient, going from regions of higher to lower 
molecular concentrations. Substance absorption into tissues 
continues until the vapor concentrations in the air space and 
tissue are the same. At this point, the system is at equilibrium 
and no further transport of substance is expected. However, 
substance entry into tissues may continue if the absorbed 
vapor is removed by transport into the bloodstream, metab-
olism to a different chemical or reaction with tissue mac-
romolecules. These processes serve to lower the substance 
concentrations in the tissue so that further diffusion into the 
tissue may again proceed. Conversely, once the vapor con-
centration is higher in tissue than the inspired air, the net 
diffusive force is external, that is, from tissue to air. Passive 
diffusion is the mode by which carbon dioxide, a byprod-
uct of aerobic metabolism, is conducted from the tissue to 
the bloodstream, to the alveolar air space, and ultimately 
removed in the expired breath.
Inhaled gases or vapors may be absorbed directly within 
the nasal cavity. While this spares the lung from direct expo-
sure, the nose is consequently exposed to high, potentially 
injurious concentrations of inhaled substances. Vapors may 
be either physically absorbed within nasal tissues, a revers-
ible process driven partly by the relative solubility of the 
vapor in air or tissue, or irreversibly bound to tissue compo-
nents. Vapors highly soluble in aqueous solutions, such as the 
mucus covering the epithelial surface of the upper respira-
tory tract, generally tend to be absorbed within these regions. 
Highly water-soluble substances, such as acetone, methanol, 
hydrogen fluoride, and propylene glycol monomethyl ether, 
are essentially completely absorbed in the nose (Morris and 
Smith 1982, Stott and McKenna 1984, Morris and Cavanagh 
1986). In contrast, gases or vapors with low water solubility, 
such as nitrogen dioxide or methylene chloride, are preferen-
tially absorbed deeper into the respiratory tract (Chang et al. 
1986, Morris 1990).
These generalizations oversimplify nasal absorption of 
vapors, but improvements in regional airflow and physiologic 
parameter measurement can allow deposition to be extrapo-
lated from animal to human results. Physiochemical and 
physiological parameters (diffusivity, tissue–air partition coef-
ficient, chemical reactivity, and metabolism) have been incor-
porated into computational fluid dynamic models of the rodent 
and human nose to predict species-dependent nasal deposition 
of vapor (Frederick et al. 2001); the results suggest that good 
agreement between rats and humans was attained with either 
one- or multi-tissue compartment models. Additional determi-
nants of vapor absorption are the upper respiratory tract vas-
cular blood flow and tissue metabolism or binding (Stott and 
McKenna 1984, Morris 1990). Irreversible binding to nasal 
tissues may occur if the inhaled vapor is reactive or is metabo-
lized within nasal epithelium, with subsequent formation of 
covalent bonds with tissue macromolecules. Such reactivity 
reduces the substance concentration in tissues and facilitates 
further diffusion and absorption.
Vapors with low chemical reactivity and water solubility 
generally bypass the nasal cavity and proceed into the con-
ductive airways. Limited absorption occurs in the conducting 
airways (CA), and since the gas exchange does not occur here, 
the CA are considered a respiratory dead space accounting 
for ~30% of the resting tidal volume (i.e., about 150 mL in 
humans). Beyond the CA, inhaled substances enter the alveo-
lar (gas exchange) region and diffuse across very thin epithe-
lial and endothelial membranes (typically 0.1 ōm thick) into 
the tissue or blood.
30.5.2  Aerosols
Deposition is the process that determines the fraction of 
inspired particles that are entrapped in the respiratory tract 
and thus fail to exit with expired air. Deposited dose equals 
the amount of inhaled material multiplied by the fraction that 
deposits. All particles that touch a surface are assumed to 
deposit at the site of initial contact. Distinct physical mech-
anisms operate on inspired particles to move them across 
streamlines of air and toward the surface of the respiratory 
tract; these are gravitational sedimentation, inertia, Brownian 
diffusion, and electrostatic forces. Which mechanisms con-
tribute most to the deposition process depend on the particles’ 
(1) aerodynamic and physicochemical characteristics, (2) the 
subject’s breathing pattern, (3) respiratory tract geometry, and 
(4) the flow and mixing pattern of the aerosol. For most aero-
sols, the first three mechanisms are most important. Detailed 
treatments on particle deposition are available (Lippmann 
1977, Brain and Valberg 1979, Heyder et al. 1980, Raabe 1982, 
Stuart 1984, Stahlhofen et al. 1989, Gerrity 1990. Tsuda et al. 
2013), as are comprehensive treatises on the physics of aerosol 
behavior (Davies 1966, Friedlander 1977, Hinds 1982, Hesketh 
1986, Willeke 1993, Tsuda et al. 2013).

1631
Inhalation Toxicology
30.5.2.1  Aerodynamic Equivalent Diameter
The transport and deposition of an inspired particle >1 ōm in 
the respiratory system are largely determined by aerodynamic 
characteristics: particle size, density, and shape plus gas veloc-
ity (Heyder 1982). A 3 ōm diameter sphere with a density of 
4 g/cm3 behaves aerodynamically the same as a 6 ōm sphere 
with a density of 1 g/cm3. Such particles may be compared by 
their aerodynamic equivalent diameter (dae). The aerodynamic 
gravitational settling velocity in the air is the particle in ques-
tion. Aerodynamic diameter is proportional to dp≈1/2, where dp 
is the geometric particle diameter and  is the particle density.
As mentioned earlier, a major factor governing the effec-
tiveness of deposition mechanisms is the size of the inspired 
particles. The importance of particle size is seen in Table 30.5, 
which lists the total and regional deposition fractions as a 
function of particle size in three healthy subjects. Regional 
deposition fraction is the fraction of the total inhaled mass that 
deposits in a defined region. For deposition studies, the typi-
cal respiratory tract regions are the oropharynx, nose, larynx, 
central airways, peripheral airways, and acini. Depending on 
particle size, the total deposition fraction is similarly sensitive 
(Heyder et al. 1986). As discussed later, selecting the correct 
particle size is one way to target particles to a desired lung 
region.
In Table 30.5, one can compare the particle size correspond-
ing to the highest deposition fraction for each lung compart-
ment. It can be seen how, during oral breathing, the larynx and 
airways act as sequential upstream filters for the acini. The 
larynx, which collects particles by impaction, consistently 
removes more particles from the airways. The laryngeal depo-
sition fraction maximum is at a dae greater than 15 ōm, since 
impaction efficiency increases with increasing dae. For the 
airways, which collect particles by impaction and sedimenta-
tion, the deposition maximum is at a dae of 7–8 ōm. Larger 
particles are removed upstream in the larynx, while smaller 
particles have lower impaction and sedimentation efficiencies. 
For the acini, which collect particles by sedimentation and 
diffusion, there are actually two maxima. The first occurs at 
a dae = 3–4 ōm. Again, larger particles are removed upstream 
in the larynx and airways, while smaller particles have lower 
sedimentation efficiencies. The second maximum occurs at 
dae < 0.006 ōm, since diffusional (Brownian) displacement 
increases with decreasing particle size. A deposition frac-
tion minimum exists at dae = 0.4 ōm, which is the particle size 
having the lowest combined settling and diffusional displace-
ments (Weiss and Stein 1993).
Our ability to estimate deposition fraction on the basis of 
particle size is confounded by the fact that most aerosols are 
polydisperse. Figure 30.13 shows typical size distributions for 
polydisperse particles produced by different mechanisms. Diu 
and Yu (1983) found that increasing polydispersity increases 
the deposition fraction for aerosols with diameters between 
0.04 and 2 ōm and decreases the deposition fraction for aero-
sols with diameters outside this range. Thus, the overall effect 
of increasing polydispersity is to decrease the dependency of 
deposition fraction on particle size.

30.5.2.1.1  Hygroscopicity and Evaporation
Many types of particles change size with time. Water drop-
lets shrink through evaporation, while hygroscopic droplets, 
such as NaCl particles, may undergo hygroscopic growth, 
especially as the relative humidity nears 100% (Cinkotai 1971, 
Ferron 1977, Ferron et al. 1988a). Since the relative humidity 
in the lungs distal to the upper airways is 99.5% (Ferron et al. 
1988a), hygroscopicity can substantially affect particle size 
and deposition fraction.
30.5.2.2  Fibers
Fibers are particles with an elongated shape (i.e., length con-
siderably greater than width) and approximately parallel sides. 
They have particular significance to human health because of 
the association of many naturally occurring fibers (e.g., asbes-
tos and erionite) and some man-made synthetic fibers, with 
pulmonary fibrosis and carcinogenesis in humans (IPCS 1986, 
Godleski 2004). Major differences in the nature and persis-
tence of lung injury have been identified based on several key 
fiber characteristics: composition, durability (which deter-
mines persistence in the lung), size (which governs penetration 
and reactivity into the lung), and exposure concentration.
For the purposes of describing the physical dimensions of 
toxic fibers and their significance, animal and occupational 
exposure studies have been done which relate size parameters 
with respiratory tract toxicity. From animal studies, a variety of 
fiber types and dimensions have been instilled or injected into 
the pleura or abdominal cavities and (Wrench 1972, Wagner 
et al. 1973, Davis 1974, Pott 1978, Kane 2006). Though these 
ways of introducing fibers into the body are not representa-
tive of normal exposure pathways, this early research provided 
fundamental insights into the physical bases for the biological 
reactivity of fibers. Based on this work, a general relationship 
between fiber dimension and lung response emerged. Notably, 
Stanton and Wrench found that intra-pleural sarcomas were 
likely, especially if the implanted fibers were >8 ōm in length 
and <1.5 ōm diameter (Stanton and Wrench 1972). In contrast, 
nonfibrous preparations of these same materials evoked a much 
less severe pulmonary response (Wright and Kuschner 1977). 
Walton (1982) has suggested that asbestos fibers with sizes of 
>5–10 ōm in length and diameters of <1.5–2 ōm with an aspect 
ratio (i.e., length-to-width ratio) of >5 are most hazardous. A 
considerable body of evidence has accumulated that very long 
and thin fibers pose the greatest health hazard. Recognizing 
that there aren’t necessarily clear distinctions between fiber 
dimension and pathogenicity, the U.S. EPA has defined a fiber 
as a particle with a length of >5 ōm with an aspect ratio of at 
least 3 (U.S. EPA 1986). In recognition of the higher hazard 
of the smaller diameter fibers, the World Health Organization 
(1985) has also stipulated a diameter of <3 ōm in their fiber 
definition.
Fiber diameter, length, and shape all influence lung depo-
sition and thus potential tissue injury. Fiber diameter is rec-
ognized as the most important factor in determining fiber 
respirability; the thinner the fiber, the more respirable it is and 
the deeper it can penetrate into the lung. The probability of 
fiber impaction and sedimentation in the respiratory tract is 
governed by the aerodynamic diameters of the fibers, which are 
approximately three times their physical diameter (Timbrell 
1965, Stober et al. 1970). Thus, a fiber with a physical diameter 
of about 3.5 ōm will behave aerodynamically as a spherical 
particle of about 10 ōm diameter, a size considered the upper 
limit of alveolar deposition. Fibers that are very thin are thus 
more likely to reach alveoli than wider fibers. In humans, the 
upper limit for aerosol deposition in the lung is considered to 
be ~10 ōm in diameter, so fibers with actual diameters >3 ōm 
FIGURE 30.13  Particle size regimes and aerosol formation mechanisms. (Adapted from Whitby 1978 with permission.)

1633
Inhalation Toxicology
are considered to be nonrespirable. Conversely, fibers with 
diameters <3 ōm but very long lengths (50–100 ōm) are highly 
respirable. Timbrell and Skidmore (1970) showed that finite 
limits exist for fiber respirability such that fibers with lengths 
exceeding ~200 ōm and diameters greater than ~3 ōm would 
not penetrate the airways to deposit in distal alveoli to any 
significant amount.
Empirical observations of fiber deposition are supported by 
modeled predictions of alveolar deposition in rats and humans 
(Dai and Yu 1998). In their studies, they found that alveolar 
deposition curves were similar in both species but maximal 
at an aerodynamic diameter of 2–3 ōm in rats (15% alveo-
lar deposition) and ~4 ōm (about 30% alveolar deposition) in 
humans. Essentially no alveolar deposition would occur in 
humans at ~10 ōm aerodynamic diameter, while for rats, the 
comparable figure would be ~5 ōm. As the fiber aspect ratio 
increased, the deposition maxima occurred at smaller aero-
dynamic diameters in both species and the extent of alveolar 
fiber deposition decreased. Lastly, fiber shape is another deter-
minant of deposition. Timbrell and Skidmore (1970) found 
that long, straight fibers tended to align their long axis in the 
direction of airflow, thus facilitating penetration into the lung. 
Fibers with curvatures, bends, or other irregularities can twist 
and rotate in the airstream, increasing the fibers’ aerodynamic 
diameter and therefore reducing respirability and deposition.
Fiber-containing test atmospheres generated for inhalation 
toxicity studies require assessment of both the fibrous and non-
fibrous components to fully characterize the test atmosphere. 
Large amounts of respirable nonfibrous dust may exacerbate 
the lung response by taxing the ability of alveolar macrophages 
to remove inhaled particles. To document exposure conditions, 
it is essential to characterize: (1) fiber composition (including 
surface modifying agents, such as binding agents), (2) length 
and width distributions of both nonfibrous and fibrous par-
ticles, and (3) air sampling data showing fiber concentration 
(typically by count, i.e., fibers/cm3). Supplemental measure-
ments can include morphology and aerodynamic particle size 
and mass. Fiber-sizing techniques involve fiber sample collec-
tion on an appropriate filter media and counting fiber numbers 
and/or length/width dimensions by using using light micros-
copy, scanning, or transmission electron microscopy..
There are three general fiber categories which are grouped 
according to their origin:
•	 naturally occurring fibers
•	 man-made synthetic mineral fibers
•	 synthetic organic fibers
The most well-known and studied naturally occurring class of 
mineral fibers is asbestos, which is grouped into two catego-
ries: (1) serpentine (e.g., chrysotile, a hydrated magnesium sili-
cate) and (2) amphibole asbestos (e.g., amosite, crocidolite, and 
tremolite, which are hydrated silicates having magnesium, sili-
con, and one or more other metallic cations). The morphology 
of these two classes is very distinct; chrysotile is a curly fiber 
that comprises parallel fibrillar subunits and amphibole fibers 
(especially amosite, a key subtype) tend to have a straight, rod-
like structure. These distinctions are important because they 
impact how pathogenic and durable these fibers are in the lung. 
Amphibole asbestos is less water soluble than chrysotile, and 
chrysotile is more likely to fragment axially, thereby releasing 
long thin fibrils of greater reactivity, whereas amphiboles are 
more likely to fragment longitudinally, releasing shorter fiber 
fragments.
Of the man-made mineral fibers, fibrous glass is perhaps 
the most studied. Unlike the naturally occurring mineral 
fibers, fibrous glass is amorphous and does not have a crystal-
line structure. Glass fibers are produced by drawing molten 
glass through a small orifice producing very small-diameter 
fibers, typically 3–10 ōm in diameter but some as fine as 1 ōm. 
Synthetic organic fibers are useful due to their high tensile 
strength and chemical or flame resistance. Para-aramid fibers, 
for example, have been used as an asbestos replacement, in 
automotive and aerospace applications and, more visibly, in 
ballistic armor. The para-aramid fibers exist as either a con-
tinuous filament or as pulped material. As initially produced, 
para-aramid fibers are too thick to deposit in alveoli, but fiber 
processing operations can physically break inter-fibril bonds, 
thus peeling away curly fibrils of sufficient size to be deeply 
respirable (Lee et al. 1988).
Fibers possess broad differences in biological reactivity in 
lung tissue that can be related to three general properties: fiber 
dimension, fiber durability (or biopersistence), and fiber dose. 
The physical dimensions of fibers determine how and where 
fibers will deposit (just as with other aerosols, the deposition 
site will impact how bioavailable the fiber is, due to site-depen-
dent processes that facilitate fiber clearance). An early clue to 
the importance of fiber length in the pathogenicity of inhaled 
fibers was the observation by Stanton and Wrench (1972) that 
fibers, regardless of composition, longer than ~8 ōm were 
more potent in the development of pulmonary mesothelial 
tumors than shorter fibers. For fibers to have any significant 
lung deposition, fiber diameters must be less than ~3 ōm, so 
the fibers most likely to be pathogenic will have diameters 
less than ~1–2 ōm. Similarly, there is an upper boundary on 
length, as fibers longer than ~200 ōm do not readily penetrate 
to peripheral alveoli in humans. Besides the ability of very 
fine diameter, high aspect ratio fibers to penetrate deeply into 
the respiratory tract, long fiber pathogenicity may determine 
whether alveolar macrophages can successfully phagocytize 
such fibers. Once fibers are longer than ~10–20 ōm, they can 
no longer be readily engulfed by macrophages and affected by 
intracellular hydrolyses. Such long fibers are thus less likely to 
be cleared by macrophages, thereby enhancing the likelihood 
of fiber retention, interaction with lung epithelium, and trans-
location to the pleura, consequently with much opportunity to 
cause disease. Collectively, the results from various in vivo and 
in vitro studies of fiber toxicity show that long thin fibers (i.e., 
fiber length > 20 ōm and diameter < 1 ōm) are the most patho-
genic in causing lung tumors (Miller et al. 1999).
Fiber durability is another key determinant of toxicity since 
fibers that resist dissolution or degradation (i.e., are biopersis-
tent) in the lung or other organ sites raise the chances for adverse 
effects. Any properties that impart chemical or mechanical 
resistance to fiber disintegration or fragmentation in the lung 
will prolong their residence in the tissue. The ability of fibers 
to disintegrate in tissues was first noted when natural and

1634
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
man-made fibers of different compositions were found to have 
different toxicological properties and lung retention character-
istics (Morgan et al. 1982, Johnson et al. 1984, Morgan 1992). 
Investigators found lower burdens of some fibers in exposed 
lungs than expected based on the fiber exposure concentrations, 
and in general, this correlated with a less severe spectrum of 
pulmonary pathologic effects (Hesterberg et al. 1998). Indeed, 
a sizable body of data has accumulated showing that the bio-
logical effects of inhaled fibers were not so much dependent on 
their composition but rather on their durability and resistance 
to disintegration in the lung (Lippmann 1990). Studies show 
that the level of biopersistence of fibers longer than 20 ōm is a 
good predictor of pathological responses following inhalation 
(Hesterberg et al. 1998, Bernstein et al. 2001).
There are two general pathways by which fiber degradation 
may proceed. In the first, the fiber simply dissolves and is com-
pletely solubilized within the lung milieu. In the second, the 
physical dimensions of the fiber (either diameter or length) may 
be altered by the action of lung fluids on the fiber surface. If sol-
ubilization alters the mechanical integrity of the fiber such that 
the fiber breaks along its longitudinal axis, both the dimensions 
and number of subsequent fibers change. If a fiber breaks trans-
versely, an additional fiber or particle is produced (depending 
upon the dimensions of the resulting fragments), and the over-
all length of the fiber is reduced; this can result in facilitated 
clearance, especially if the fiber fragments now have a size that 
may be readily engulfed and removed from the lung by mac-
rophages. Conversely, some fiber types are especially prone to 
mechanical disruption, releasing more fibers. Chrysotile asbes-
tos deposited in the lung, for example, may split longitudinally, 
releasing numerous fine fibrils and increasing the lung fiber 
burden, as demonstrated by Bellmann et al. (1986). Similarly, 
para-aramid fibers can be disrupted transversely at fibril defect 
sites by an enzymatic attack to release shorter fibers (Kelly et 
al. 1993, Warheit et al. 2006). Dissolution processes that affect 
fiber length are more likely to reduce potential adverse lung 
effects by allowing phagocytosis of fiber fragments and alveo-
lar clearance. The various factors influencing fiber durability 
have been reviewed by Searl (1994), and experimental ways to 
evaluate fiber solubility in vitro and in vivo have been reviewed 
by Hesterberg et al. (1996, 1998).
Experimental demonstration of fiber degradation in vivo has 
been reported by measuring the dimensions of fibers recovered 
from the lungs of animals. Techniques vary, but common lung 
digestion methods utilize either thermal or microwave ashing 
techniques, chemical digestion with strong alkali, or oxidizing 
agents (Kelly et al. 1985, Warheit et al. 1991) followed by stan-
dard fiber size/count techniques. It is imperative to determine 
the effects of the lung digestion method on the fibers alone to 
ensure that artifactual reductions in fiber size or number do 
not occur. This consideration, along with the need for tissue 
destruction, may be bypassed with in situ observations of fiber 
dimensions by using histological techniques and scanning or 
TEM (Brody et al. 1981).
30.5.2.3  Particle Size and Mass Distributions
To estimate total and regional lung doses, it is necessary 
to characterize the aerosol in terms of the particle size 
distribution and the mass concentration. Particle size is a 
key factor governing: (1) the fraction of inhaled particles that 
can penetrate past the oropharynx and enter the lungs and (2) 
the particle fraction that deposits in each lung region. The 
mass concentration is proportional to the quantity of par-
ticles that deposits in each region. Size distributions can be 
described in terms of particle number, surface area, or mass; 
most dose estimates use mass. Particle mass distributions 
are often characterized by two values: the MMAD [MMAD 
(dae, mm)] and the GSD [GSD (σg)] (Heyder 1991). The dae, 
mm denotes the particle size at which half of the total aero-
sol mass is contained in larger particles and half in smaller 
particles. Since the dae, mm is expressed as an aerodynamic 
diameter, it describes how the overall aerosol behaves in the 
respired air and can be used to estimate where and by what 
processes particles deposit in the respiratory tract. Another 
frequently encountered particle size parameter is the mass 
median diameter (dmm or MMD), which is the same as the 
dae, mm except that the particle size is expressed in terms of the 
particle’s actual density (Figure 30.15).
The σg denotes the spread of particle sizes. Most aerosols 
have particle sizes that are distributed lognormally; that is, on 
a plot of frequency distribution versus log particle diameter, 
the distribution looks Gaussian. An aerosol with all identically 
sized particles has a σg of 1.0. An aerosol with a σg ≥ 1.22 is 
considered monodisperse (Fuchs and Sutugin 1981): to a first 
approximation, all the particles behave aerodynamically alike. 
An aerosol with a σg > 1.22 is polydisperse: there are apprecia-
ble differences in aerodynamic behavior among the particles. 
Most therapeutic aerosols are polydisperse. An aerosol with a 
dae, mm of 2 ōm and a σg of 2 has ±1 GSD or 68% of its mass con-
tained between particles of 1 and 4 ōm. An important implica-
tion of a lognormal distribution is that most of the aerosol mass 
is contained in the large particles since mass is proportional 
to the cube of diameter. This means that where these large 
particles deposit in the respiratory tract governs where much 
of the dose deposits.
30.5.2.4  Aerosol Deposition Mechanisms
The major mechanisms of particle deposition are shown in 
Figure 30.14.
30.5.2.4.1  Gravitational Settling
Gravity accelerates falling bodies downward, and a particle 
reaches its terminal settling velocity when the gravitational 
force equals the opposing viscous resistive forces of the air. 
Particles small enough to enter the lungs reach their terminal 
settling velocity in less than 0.1 ms. Particles deposit when 
they have enough time to settle onto airway walls or alveolar 
surfaces. Thus, the probability that a particle deposit by gravi-
tational settling increases with increasing dae2t, where t is the 
residence time in the respiratory tract (Heyder 1982). Breath 
holding enhances deposition by sedimentation. Sedimentation 
is important for particles larger than about 0.1 ōm that enter 
the peripheral airways and alveoli, where airflow rates are 
slow, residence times are long, and distances to lung surfaces 
are short (Heyder et al. 1986).

1635
Inhalation Toxicology
30.5.2.4.2  Inertial Impaction
Inertia is the tendency of a moving particle to resist changes in 
speed and direction. It is related to momentum, the product of 
the particle’s mass and velocity. High air velocities and abrupt 
changes in airflow direction occur in the nose and oropharynx 
and at central airway bifurcations. Inertia causes a particle 
entering bends at these sites to continue in its original direction 
instead of following the curvature of the airflow. If the particle 
has sufficient mass and velocity, it crosses airflow streamlines 
and impacts the airway wall. The probability that a particle 
deposit by inertial impaction increases with increasing prod-
uct of dae2, where Q is the respired flow rate (Lippmann and 
Albert 1969, Heyder 1982). Impaction probably also increases 
as the angle of airstream deflection increases. Inertial impac-
tion is an important deposition mechanism for particles with a 
dae > 2 ōm (Heyder 1982) and may occur during both inspira-
tion and expiration in the extrathoracic airways (oropharynx, 
nasopharynx, and larynx) and central airways. In obstructed 
airways, the enhanced linear velocities at narrowed sites may 
cause smaller particles to deposit by impaction as well.
30.5.2.4.3  Diffusion
Aerosol particles also undergo Brownian diffusion, a random 
motion in three dimensions caused by their collisions with gas 
molecules; this motion can lead to contact and deposition on 
respiratory surfaces. Diffusion is significant for particles with 
diameters less than 1 ōm, so that their size is similar to the 
mean free path of gas molecules. Unlike inertial or gravita-
tional displacement, diffusion is independent of airflow rate 
and particle density; however, it is affected by particle shape 
and size (Heyder and Scheuch 1983). For diffusing particles 
that measure 0.4 ōm and smaller, size can be expressed in terms 
of the thermodynamic equivalent diameter (dte), the diameter 
of a sphere that has the same average diffusional velocity in 
air as the actual particle (Heyder 1991). The probability that 
a particle deposits by diffusion increases with decreasing par-
ticle size and decreasing values of (t/dte)1/2 (Heyder 1982); thus, 
deposition is dependent on the square root of residence time. 
Diffusion, like sedimentation, is usually most important in the 
peripheral airways and alveoli (Heyder et al. 1986) but can 
also occur in the nose and mouth (Gradon and Yu 1989).
As particle size decreases, inertia and sedimentation 
become less important, but diffusion increases in importance. 
For example, a particle with a dae of 2 ōm moves by diffusion 
(Brownian displacement) only about 9 ōm in 1 second; it settles 
by gravity about 125 ōm in the same period. However, as parti-
cle size drops to 0.2 ōm, the diffusional displacement increases 
to 37 ōm, whereas gravitational displacement decreases to 
only 2.1 ōm. Generally, gravity and inertia dominate the trans-
port and deposition of particles larger than 1.0 ōm in diameter, 
whereas diffusion dominates the transport of particles smaller 
than 0.1 ōm. For particles between 0.1 and 1.0 ōm, sedimenta-
tion and diffusion are both important (Heyder and Scheuch 
1983, Heyder 1991).
30.5.2.4.4  Electrostatic Attraction
Electrical forces may cause charged particles to deposit in 
the respiratory tract. The surfaces of the respiratory tract are 
uncharged but electrically conducting. When an electrically 
charged particle approaches the lung surface, the particle 
induces on that surface an image charge of the opposite polar-
ity that attracts the particle. This attraction may cause the par-
ticle to deposit, and thus the deposition of charged particles 
relative to that of neutral particles is increased. Experimental 
(Melandri et al. 1983) and theoretical (Yu 1985) studies show 
that electrostatic attraction may be an important deposition 
mechanism in the lung periphery for particles (especially 
fibers) that are charged above a certain threshold and have a 
diameter of 0.1–1.0 ōm (Vincent 1985). Melandri et al. (1983) 
established that the probability of electrostatic deposition is 
proportional to (q2/d)1/3, where q is the electrical charge on the 
particle. For charged 1 ōm particles, deposition is increased 
over that of uncharged particles once there are about 40 charges 
on the particle; for 0.3 ōm particles, only 10 charges are needed.
30.5.2.4.5  Interception and Other Forces
Deposition can also occur in the lungs when particles have 
dimensions that are significant relative to those of the air 
spaces. As particles move into smaller and smaller air spaces, 
some may reach a point where the distance to a surface from 
the center of a particle is less than the particle size. The result-
ing contact is called interception. Interception is particularly 
important for the deposition of fibers (Timbrell 1965, Harris 
and Fraser 1976). Other forces acting to affect deposition, such 
as acoustic forces, magnetic forces, or thermal forces, are nor-
mally not significant in the respiratory tract.
The physical mechanisms responsible for deposition have 
already been discussed. The effectiveness of these mechanisms 
depends on various factors such as particle size, breathing pat-
tern, airway geometry, and disease. Many aspects of particle 
deposition in the respiratory tract have captured the energy 
and imagination of investigators; these studies are reviewed in 
many papers and books (Lippmann 1977, Brain and Valberg 
1979, Heyder et al. 1982, 1986, Raabe 1982, Agnew 1984, 
Stuart 1984, Stahlhofen et al. 1989, Gerrity 1990, Vincent 
FIGURE 30.14  Mechanisms of particle deposition.

1636
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
1990). Other excellent sources of information on deposition 
are published works from the International Symposia on 
Inhaled Particles (Davies 1967, 1961, Stuart et al. 1971, Wright 
and Kuschner1977, Dodgson 1985, Dodgson et al. 1988). Some 
major influences on the fraction of the inspired particles that 
deposit within the respiratory tract and the anatomical distri-
bution of retained particles are now discussed (Figure 30.15).
30.5.2.5  Effectiveness of Deposition Mechanisms
Some major influences on the fraction of inspired particles that 
deposit within the respiratory tract and the anatomical distri-
bution of retained particles are now discussed. Figure 30.15 is 
a diagrammatical representation of these concepts.
30.5.2.5.1  Anatomy of the Respiratory Tract
The configuration of the lungs and airways is important 
because the efficiency of deposition depends in part on the 
diameters of the airways, their angles of branching, and the 
average distances of particles to lung surfaces in the acini. 
Along with the volumetric flow rate, airway anatomy speci-
fies the local linear velocity of the airstream and thus deter-
mines whether the flow is laminar or turbulent. For example, 
at the laryngeal aperture, an air jet is formed that creates 
turbulent and unstable airflows that enhance particle impac-
tion in the trachea (Lippmann and Altshuler 1976, Chan 
et al. 1980). There are interspecies and intraspecies differ-
ences in lung morphometry (Soong et al. 1979, Schlesinger 
and McFadden 1981, Nikiforov and Schlesinger 1985); even 
within the same individual, the dimensions of the respira-
tory tract change with lung volume, age, and pathological 
processes. Among normal subjects breathing in the same 
manner, the total deposition fraction has a coefficient of vari-
ation as large as 27%. This mostly stems from intersubject 
differences in airway geometry (Heyder et al. 1982, 1988, Yu 
and Diu 1982, Blanchard et al. 1991). Gender differences in 
laryngeal and airway geometries may cause women to have 
greater upper airway deposition by impaction compared to 
men (Pritchard et al. 1986, Svartengren et al. 1991). For any 
person, decreasing lung volume not only increases the depo-
sition fraction but also causes the major site of particle depo-
sition within the airways to shift from the lung periphery to 
more central airways (Agnew 1984). At low lung volumes, 
airways have smaller cross-sectional areas and higher linear 
velocities and so there is more deposition by impaction in 
central airways at a given flow rate. As a person ages, the 
anatomy of the respiratory tract changes, which also affects 
deposition fraction (Phalen et al. 1991). Children under 
8 years old who are breathing at rest have higher total, head, 
and tracheobronchial deposition fractions and lower alveolar 
deposition fraction than adults (Xu and Yu 1986, Yu and Xu 
1987, Hofmann et al. 1989b, Becquemin et al. 1991b). The 
higher airway deposition may help explain why some chil-
dren have a greater response than adults to a given dose of a 
provocation agent (LeSouef 1992).
30.5.2.5.2  Choice of Pathway
Most people breathe nasally at rest. As the ventilation rate 
rises above 35–40 L/minute, the high-resistance nasal path-
way starts to be combined with the low-resistance oral path-
way (Saibene et al. 1978, Niinimaa et al. 1980, Chadha et al. 
1987). With continuing increases in ventilation rate, the oral 
path accounts for a greater percentage of the total ventila-
tion, but this varies among subjects, ranging from only 10% 
to as much as 80% (Niinimaa et al. 1981, Chadha et al. 1987, 
Wheatley et al. 1991).
The effective anatomy of the respiratory tract changes in a 
highly impactful way when air is inhaled through the mouth 
instead of the nose, or when the nose is bypassed because a 
tracheostomy or an endotracheal tube is present. The nose 
plays an important role as an upstream filter that prevents 
inhaled particles from penetrating into the lungs. The smallest 
total cross-sectional area of the respiratory tract also occurs 
in the nose. High velocities, sharp curves, nasal hairs, and 
FIGURE 30.15  ICRP model of regional aerosol deposition in humans.

1637
Inhalation Toxicology
secondary flows all combine to collect inhaled particles in the 
nose. Excellent reviews of particle inhalability and deposition 
in the nose are available (e.g., Hounam and Morgan [1977], 
Lippmann [1977], Yu et al. [1981], Sato [1988], Stahlhofen 
et al. [1989], Vincent [1990], Martonen and Zhang [1992]). The 
inhalability, deposition, and clearance of particles in the head 
during nasal breathing have been studied by several investi-
gators (e.g., Pattle [1961], Lippmann [1970], Hounam [1971], 
Heyder and Rudolf [1977], Yu et al. [1981], Heyder et al. [1986], 
Schiller et al. [1988], Breysse and Swift [1990], Rasmussen 
et al. [1990], Becquemin et al. [1991a]). Collectively, these 
reports indicate that the anterior portion of the nose collects 
particles larger than 1 ōm by impaction, with the probability of 
impaction increasing with increasing

, though some evi-
dence indicates that impaction probability is better described

, where p is the pressure drop across the nose 
(Hounam 1971, Stahlhofen et al. 1989). Most studies show that 
particles larger than 10 ōm are completely trapped in the nose. 
The actual fraction of inhaled material trapped in the nose var-
ies considerably among individuals and is more variable than 
the deposition fraction in the lungs (Rasmussen et al. 1990). 
These intersubject differences may come from variations in 
nasal distensibility and geometry, including the number and 
shape of nasal hairs.
Besides being an upstream filter, the nose also humidi-
fies the inspired air; it is a more effective humidifier than the 
mouth because the inhaled air passes by the large surface 
area of the highly vascular nasal turbinates. Because of this 
humidification, hygroscopic particles (dae > 1 ōm) grow more 
rapidly when inhaled through the nose than when through the 
mouth and thus have enhanced deposition fraction (Ferron 
et al. 1988b).
The nose is an efficient filter for large particles, but the 
mouth, because of its simpler geometry, is not. The shift from 
a higher filtering efficiency during purely nasal breathing to 
a lower filtering efficiency during oral-nasal breathing may 
potentiate asthma attacks during exercise. During exercise, 
large volumes of air are inhaled by the mouth, so that more 
and more antigenic particles, such as pollens, penetrate to and 
deposit in the lungs. Besides increasing the risk of allergic 
responses, this exercise-induced higher exposure of airways 
can result in more particles being deposited than are predicted 
by the increased ventilation.
30.5.2.5.3  Breathing Pattern: Flow Rate and Lung Volume
When subjects change breathing pattern, deposition fractions 
may change profoundly. Minute volume determines the total 
number of particles entering the lungs. Flow rate affects the 
probability of particle impaction and the extent of turbulent 
flow, which enhances particle deposition in the upper airways. 
The combination of flow rate, tidal volume, breath-holding 
time, and lung volume affects particle residence time in each 
lung region and hence the probability of deposition by gravi-
tational and diffusional forces (Heyder 1982). Tidal volume 
and flow rate also influence laryngeal motion, and so affect 
particle deposition there. Changing lung volume also alters 
the dimensions of the airways and parenchyma. High levels 
of ventilation and breath holding represent extremes of breath-
ing patterns; they give rise to markedly different deposition 
patterns.
Generally, when airflow velocities increase, as during physi-
cal exertion or labored respiration consequent to an asthma 
attack, inertial deposition increases. During slow, relaxed 
breathing, particle sedimentation and diffusion are the pri-
mary deposition processes, and particle collection in the acini 
increases because the lung surfaces lie in close proximity 
there.
30.5.2.5.4  Species Differences
Human responses to inhaled gases and particles are often pre-
dicted from experiments done in other species, such as rodents 
or dogs (Brain et al. 1988). Besides obvious differences in 
body mass, anatomy, and lifespan, differences in metabolism 
between species are often substantial. For example, the oxygen 
consumption in rats per kilogram of body weight is 5.5 times 
greater than that of humans (Badeer 1974). Other important 
differences, such as nocturnal activity or coprophagy, may be 
important and may influence responses to inhaled toxic agents. 
Despite these differences, it is often necessary to extrapolate 
experimental results from animals to humans (Table 30.1).
All aspects of this chapter, in fact, are influenced by spe-
cies differences. Naturally, this includes deposition, clearance, 
and the magnitude and type of biological responses. A major 
difference, which is sometimes not recognized, is that rodents 
and dogs are obligatory nose breathers, while humans, particu-
larly with increased exercise and ventilation, switch from nose 
breathing to mouth breathing. This has a profound influence 
on the concentration of particles and gases reaching airways.
30.5.2.5.5  Changes in Deposition Pattern with 
Pre-existing Lung Disease
Variations in deposition with pre-existing disease have impli-
cations for using inhaled therapeutic aerosols as well as for 
the development of further disease, especially when airborne 
inhalants have contributed to the disease. Pulmonary fibrosis, 
chronic bronchitis, emphysema, and asthma have been studied. 
Inhaled particle deposition in animal models of pulmonary 
fibrosis resulted in increased deposition away from the most 
diseased areas of the lung, i.e., more deposition in the com-
paratively normal alveolated lung tissue (Sweeney et al. 1983). 
Using an animal model of emphysema, deposition was shown 
to be increased in small airways and was decreased in the 
large air spaces with emphysematous changes (Sweeney et al. 
1987). Pre-existing chronic bronchitis resulted in much greater 
particle deposition in the CA of this animal model (Sweeney 
et al. 1995). In human asthmatic subjects, total UFP deposition 
was greater than in normal subjects (Chalupa et al. 2004). Also 
in human asthmatics, test particle deposition in central air-
ways was greater than in the peripheral lung (Lay et al. 2009, 
Bennett et al. 2011). Application of computational modeling of 
particle deposition in lungs with chronic obstructive pulmo-
nary disease has also received recent attention (Ganguly et al. 
2019). Such deposition research has generated more interest as 
inhaled therapeutic options have become more common, and it 
is likely to be an important focus of future research.

1638
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
30.5.2.6  Particle Clearance Mechanisms 
in Airways and Parenchyma
The lungs’ response to particles depends not only on how 
many particles are deposited but also on the amount retained 
over time. Retention is the amount of material present in the 
lungs at any time and equals deposited mass minus cleared 
mass. During continuous aerosol exposure, the lungs would be 
expected to reach a steady state of retention when the rate of 
deposition equals the rate of clearance. It is both the amount of 
particles retained over time within a specific lung region and 
the physicochemical properties of the retained particles that 
determine the magnitude of the lungs’ response.
As discussed previously, such factors as particle size, hygro-
scopicity, and breathing pattern affect where inhaled mate-
rial deposits in the respiratory tract. In turn, the deposition 
site in the lungs determines which mechanisms are used to 
clear them, how fast they are cleared, and the amount retained 
over time. The implications of particle characteristics for inte-
grated retention are described by Brain and Valberg (1974) 
on the basis of the model developed by the Task Group on 
Lung Dynamics (Bates et al. 1966). They show that the total 
amount and the distribution of retained dose among the nose 
and pharynx, trachea and bronchi, and pulmonary and lym-
phatic compartments are dramatically altered by particle size 
and solubility.
Particles that deposit on the ciliated airways are cleared pri-
marily by the mucociliary escalator. Those particles that pen-
etrate to and deposit in the peripheral, nonciliated lung spaces 
can be cleared by varied mechanisms, including phagocyto-
sis by alveolar macrophages, particle dissolution, or move-
ment of free particles or particle-containing cells directly 
into the interstitium or the lymphatics. Excellent reviews 
discuss these clearance processes in detail (Lippmann et al. 
1980, Schlesinger 1985, Cuddihy and Yeh 1988, Oberdorster 
1988, Byron and Phillips 1990, King 1990, Vincent 1990, 
Bustamante-Marin and Ostrowski 2017, Benam et al. 2018, 
Vanaki et al. 2020).
30.5.2.6.1  Mucociliary Transport Physiology
The ciliated epithelial cells of the airways and nasal passages 
are covered by a two-layered mucous blanket: a top high-vis-
cosity gel layer and a bottom low-viscosity serous layer. The 
gel–mucous blanket, along with any less soluble particles that 
deposit on it, is moved toward the pharynx by the cilia. Also 
present in this moving mucous carpet are cells and particles 
that have been transported from the nonciliated alveoli to the 
ciliated airways. Mucus, cells, and debris coming from the 
nasal cavities and the lungs meet at the pharynx, mix with 
salivary secretions, are swallowed, and enter the gastrointes-
tinal tract. In humans, the ciliated epithelium extends from 
the trachea down to the terminal bronchioles. The particles 
are removed within half-times of minutes to hours; the rate 
depends on the speed of the mucous blanket. The speed is 
faster in the trachea than in the small airways (Serafini et al. 
1976) and is affected by factors influencing either the cilia 
or the amount and quality of mucus. In the airways, there is 
little time for slowly dissolving materials to be solubilized. In 
contrast, particles deposited in the nonciliated compartments 
have much longer residence times; there, small differences in 
solubility can result in significantly different clearance rates.
Ciliary action may be affected by many factors:
•	 number of strokes per minute
•	 amplitude of each stroke
•	 time course and form of each stroke
•	 length of the cilia
•	 ratio of ciliated to nonciliated area
•	 susceptibility of the cilia to intrinsic and extrinsic 
agents that modify their rate and quality of motion.
The characteristics of the mucous layer are critically important. 
The thickness of the mucous layer and its rheological proper-
ties may vary widely. In asthmatic subjects, tracheobronchial 
clearance can be retarded compared to normal subjects, pos-
sibly because of both ciliary dysfunction and altered mucous 
rheological properties (Mezey et al. 1978). In the peripheral 
bronchioles, for example, the gel layer is less evident. Another 
factor influencing mucociliary clearance in asthmatics is the 
presence or absence of flow-limiting segments during tidal 
breathing (O’Riordan et al. 1992). Flow-limiting segments may 
mechanically disturb the local mucociliary layer, thus slowing 
clearance (Foster et al. 1988). Asthmatics without flow-lim-
iting segments may have normal or even faster-than-normal 
mucociliary clearance rates (O’Riordan et al. 1992). Aging 
also reduces mucociliary clearance (Lowery et al. 2013). Many 
of the factors that influence mucociliary clearance, and their 
clinical implications, have been usefully reviewed (Wanner 
1977, Camner and Mossberg 1988, Satir and Sleigh 1991).
30.5.2.6.2  Measuring Mucociliary Transport
Mucociliary transport has been studied by a variety of tech-
niques, such as monitoring the movement of inert or radio-
labeled particles deposited on the tracheal mucus via either a 
bronchoscope or an inhaled bolus. One can estimate tracheal 
mucous velocity from the distance the particles move over 
time, as observed either with movies filmed through the bron-
choscope or with a gamma camera (Sackner et al. 1973, Yeates 
et al. 1975). Bronchoscopic techniques yield higher numbers 
for tracheal mucous velocity (15–21 mm/minutes) than do the 
noninvasive bolus techniques (4.4 mm/minutes). These values 
describe only the trachea and large central airways. In con-
trast, terminal bronchiolar transport is about 0.1–0.6 mm/min-
ute (Morrow et al. 1967).
Many investigators have estimated mucociliary transport 
from whole-lung clearance curves. These curves are generated 
by first having a subject inhale a radiolabeled aerosol and then 
monitoring the amount of radioactivity in the lungs over time 
(hours to days). Albert and Arnett (1955) first used this method 
and described two phases of the clearance curve, a fast and a 
slow phase. The fast phase is complete within 24–48 hours and 
has generally been attributed to tracheobronchial clearance; the 
slow phase has been attributed to alveolar clearance (Booker 
et al. 1967, Morrow et al. 1967, Lippmann and Albert 1969). This 
approach has been used to study clearance in normal and abnor-
mal subjects (Lourenco et al. 1971, Sanchis et al. 1972, Poe et al. 
1977, Camner and Philipson 1978, Stahlhofen et al. 1980).

1639
Inhalation Toxicology
There is evidence that clearance from the airways may be 
incomplete in the first 24 hours; this may be even more pro-
nounced in patients with lung disease. Gore and Patrick (1982) 
noted that particles instilled into the trachea can be seques-
tered in epithelial cells. Geiser et al. (1990) found latex par-
ticles trapped in the periciliary sol fluid below the gel–mucous 
blanket. The particles may have been displaced there by sur-
face tension forces created by a surfactant (Schurch et al. 1990, 
Gehr et al. 1991). Stahlhofen et al. (1986) noted both fast and 
slow phases of clearance even in humans given a bolus of par-
ticles delivered only 45 mL beyond the larynx. Wolff et al. 
(1989) delivered radiolabeled particles with a bronchoscope to 
airway generations 6–10 in dogs and found that about 20% of 
the particles were cleared slowly.
Another approach to measuring mucociliary clearance is to 
examine the deposition and clearance of particles in central 
versus peripheral lung regions (Smaldone et al. 1988), which 
is referred to as the aerosol penetrance or the airways penetra-
tion index (Dolovich et al. 1976, Agnew 1991). The appropri-
ate interpretation of such clearance curves has been described 
(Foster 1988, Agnew 1991).
Recently, a detailed review of the advantages and limita-
tions of the current technique used to measure mucus secre-
tion, composition, and mucociliary transport was carried out 
by Atanasova and Reznikov (2019). Also, advances in the 
mathematical modeling of mucociliary clearance have been 
reviewed (Xu and Jiang 2019). Despite many advances, there 
is still a need to better understand its role and importance 
in patients with pulmonary disease. Clearly, it is essential to 
understand the initial deposition pattern of inhaled particles 
to determine the importance of mucociliary processes for the 
clearance of particles from the lungs.
30.5.2.6.3  Nonciliated Regions: Alveolar Macrophages
Particles that deposit in the nonciliated portion of the lungs 
may dissolve in extracellular fluids if soluble or if of lesser sol-
ubility are ingested by alveolar macrophages then cleared by 
intracellular dissolution, or by movement of macrophages to 
the mucociliary escalator, or a combination of these processes. 
The process by which most particles move toward the ciliated 
region within alveolar macrophages is termed mechanical 
clearance. It is possible that particles also move as a result of 
a surface tension gradient due to surfactant that extends from 
the alveoli to the airways (Rensch et al. 1983). Other particles 
may enter the interstitial tissues, particularly when deposited 
levels are high (Lehnert 1990). Macrophages are credited with 
keeping the alveolar surfaces clean and sterile. These cells rest 
on the continuous epithelial layer of the lung. It is their phago-
cytic and lytic capabilities that provide most of the bactericidal 
properties of the lungs. Uptake of unopsonized particles is via 
scavenger receptors on alveolar macrophage cell membranes 
(Kobzik 1995). Rapid endocytosis prevents particle penetration 
through the alveolar epithelia and facilitates alveolar–bron-
chiolar transport. Particle clearance by alveolar macrophages 
was the focus of an international symposium (Morgan 1992). 
Macrophages can be found in distinct locations in the lung 
including alveoli, airway, connective tissue, pleura, and intra-
vascular macrophages (Reviewed by Brain 1988). In addition, 
the biology of lung macrophages has been usefully reviewed 
(Brain 1985, Herscowitz 1985, Du Bois 1986, Fels and Cohn 
1986, Bowden 1987, Sibelle and Reynolds 1990, Reynolds 
1991, Valberg and Blanchard 1991, Hiraiwa and van Eeden 
2013, Tschernig et al. 2016, Baharom et al. 2017).
Particles enter the lung connective tissue by translocation 
through type I epithelial cells (Adamson and Bowden 1981), 
though some data indicate that alveolar macrophages carry par-
ticles into the interstitium as well (Harmsen et al. 1985). Once in 
the interstitium, most particles are phagocytized by interstitial 
macrophages, though some particles may temporarily remain 
free. Interstitial particles are cleared eventually through either 
dissolution (Kreyling et al. 1991) or transportation to regional 
lymph nodes through lymphatic pathways. In tissues, particles 
are cleared with half-times ranging from a few days to thou-
sands of days, depending on their solubility. In contrast, par-
ticles remaining on alveolar surfaces in humans are cleared with 
biological half-times estimated to be hours to months. Lung 
macrophage clearance can be slowed by the toxicity of the par-
ticles taken up by phagocytosis; alpha quart is a prime example 
of this phenomenon (Kawasaki 2019). Table 30.6 summarizes 
particle clearance mechanisms in the respiratory tract by ana-
tomic region with approximate clearance half-times.
30.5.2.7  Measuring Deposition, 
Clearance, and Retention
A concept essential to inhalation toxicology is the idea of a 
dose–response curve. Implicit in this concept is quantifying 
retention (the amount of an aerosol present at any time). It 
should be remembered that retention is different from depo-
sition (initial attachment of suspended particles to a surface 
in the respiratory tract). As discussed elsewhere, retention but 
not deposition is influenced by clearance and translocation. In 
the next paragraph, we briefly mention strategies to measure 
retention in animals and humans.

Interstitial migration
Months
Alveolar
Dissolution
Hours to months
