Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
There are obvious advantages to using nondestructive, non-
invasive methods to quantify particle retention. Commonly 
used technologies include gamma cameras, magnetometry, 
and positron emission tomography (PET) scans. An advan-
tage is that repeated measurements in the same human or 
animal are possible. Although spatial resolution has improved 
(Sanchez-Crespo 2019), more precise results can be obtained 
by analyzing post-mortem animal subjects where it is possible 
to divide the respiratory tract into pieces and analyze the par-
ticle content of each piece. For example, Kreyling et al. (1999) 
combined radioactive and fluorescent particle studies in canine 
subjects to better understand clearance in the small airways. 
In addition to using particles that are radioactive, fluorescent, 
radio-opaque, or magnetic, other particles, such as iron oxide 
or talc, may have characteristic visual appearances that facili-
tate identification by light or electron microscopy (Sato et al. 
2020).
It is also possible to estimate initial deposited dose based 
on measurements of the size and concentration of the aerosol, 
as well as breathing pattern. Numerous models for predicting 
deposition have been described. Another approach is simply to 
measure the difference in the aerosol content between inspired 
and expired gas, that is, its disappearance as well as ventila-
tion. Then one can determine the total amount of material 
initially deposited in the respiratory tract. However, no infor-
mation is obtained regarding the anatomic location.
30.6  Inhalation Toxicology: Biological Responses
30.6.1  Biological Mechanisms of 
Particle and Gas Injury
Evidence indicates that fine particles are inherently more toxic 
than larger particles. Proposed hypotheses for fine particle-
induced injury include (1) reductions in the integrity of the 
pulmonary epithelial and/or endothelial barriers, (2) impaired 
pulmonary host defense mechanisms, (3) release of inflam-
matory mediators to produce either local or systemic effects, 
(4) impaired particle clearance with airway hypersecretion 
of mucus, (5) aggravation of pre-existing airway occlusion, 
and (6) direct or indirect effects on cardiovascular function 
(Castranova 1998). While all appear to be involved to vary-
ing degrees, particle-mediated inflammation may well be the 
predominant mechanism for respiratory tract injury and car-
diovascular effects.
Inhaled particles can be directly cytotoxic, effecting transu-
dation of serum proteins from alveolar capillaries. This may 
involve peroxidation of membrane lipids, resulting in defects 
in membrane integrity, function, and, subsequently, cell death; 
if injury is sufficiently severe, the airways and alveoli fill with 
fluid, causing pulmonary edema and ultimately death. The 
source of these radicals may be membrane-based oxidases 
(Vallyathan et al. 1992).
Some metal-containing particles, especially those that either 
contain or can complex the ferric form (Fe+3) of iron, are par-
ticularly active in inducing membrane peroxidation through 
a reduction–oxidation pathway, producing hydroxyl radical 
(OH) (Ghio et al. 1992b); inclusion of chelators markedly 
reduces the oxidant-generating capacity of the particles (Ghio 
et al. 1992a). This pathway may be especially important for 
nanoscale materials as these particles may have a dispropor-
tionately larger number of catalytic sites on the surface relative 
to larger particles (Tran et al. 2000, Duffin et al. 2002). By 
a related mechanism, particles, either by themselves (Wilson 
et al. 2002) or by interaction with pulmonary alveolar mac-
rophages or alveolar type II cells (Kanj et al. 2005), pulmo-
nary epithelium (Shukla et al. 2000), or polymorphonuclear 
leukocytes (Prahalad et al. 1999), can stimulate the generation 
of reactive oxidant species (ROS) such as hydrogen peroxide 
(H2O2), superoxide anion (O2−), or hydroxyl radical (Gurgueira 
et al. 2002, Gonzalez-Flecha 2004, Ghelfi et al. 2008, Rhoden 
et al. 2008). These radicals may cause either direct tissue 
injury or genetic damage. A variety of metal-containing dusts 
have been shown to stimulate production of ROS from mac-
rophages (Berg et al. 1993), with iron being among the most 
active metals (Fubini et al. 1995, Rice et al. 2001), while other 
metal dusts, such as titanium dioxide, are either inactive or 
minimally active (Goodglick and Kane 1986, Nyberg and 
Klockars 1990, Blackford et al. 1994).
Oxidant production, whether caused by fine or UFPs, may 
also oxidize and deplete glutathione, a cellular antioxidant 
(Stone et al. 1998). The relative levels of reduced and oxi-
dized glutathione appear to play a pivotal role in determin-
ing the lung’s inflammatory response to oxidant stresses. 
Alterations in the redox levels of glutathione can affect the 
degree of acetylation/deacetylation of histone proteins on 
DNA and allow transcription to occur (Rahman et al. 2002). 
Conformational changes in DNA allow access to the nuclear 
transcription factors (nuclear factor-kappa B [NF-✓B]) and 
the associated activation protein (Galter et al. 1994, Haddad 
et al. 2000), permitting gene transcription. Studies with sin-
gle-walled carbon nanotubes (SWNT) in vitro, for example, 
show cells undergoing oxidant stress with a decreased ratio of 
reduced/oxidized glutathione, thereby triggering activation of 
the AP-1 and NF-✓B nuclear transcription factors (Dick et al. 
2003, Manna et al. 2005). Gene transcription results in the 
production of various proinflammatory cytokines, a class of 
chemical messengers that regulate cellular homeostasis and 
cell proliferation. Notably, the initiating or proinflammatory 
cytokines, such as tumor necrosis factor- (TNF-) and inter-
leukin-1 (IL-1), can induce production of other cytokines that 
are responsible for the recruitment of inflammatory cells such 
as neutrophils to sites of particle deposition (Driscoll 1996). 
As a consequence, such recruited cells are activated to pro-
duce ROS, causing further toxic insult to airway epithelium 
integrity.
Noncytotoxic, poorly soluble particles can lead to tissue 
injury from the prolonged inflammatory response induced by 
high particle concentrations (either from high exposure levels 
or inability to remove inhaled particles) in the lung (Morrow et 
al. 1996). This phenomenon, known as lung overload, can pro-
duce similar pathologic lesions in the lung as more cytotoxic 
dusts such as quartz (Oberdorster 1988) but requires much 
higher lung burdens than with quartz. Lung overload arises 
when the normal clearance processes become less and less 
effective with high particle exposures, leading to accumulation 
of particles within the lungs (Morrow and Mermelstein 1988).

1641
Inhalation Toxicology
Pathologically, overload appears initially as an increased num-
ber of particles in the interstitium in macrophages in air spaces; 
with time, chronic inflammation may ensue, resulting in alveo-
lar cell hyperplasia, granulomas, fibrosis, and potentially lung 
tumors (Morrow and Mermelstein 1988). Lung tumors have 
not been reported if particle clearance and pulmonary inflam-
mation do not occur, suggesting a threshold-related phenom-
enon for overload-related lung tumors.
The functional decrements in clearance due to dust over-
loading are apparent if the clearance kinetics are measured; 
overloaded lungs exhibited a marked increase in particle 
retention compared to normal lungs. The mechanism for 
the overload phenomenon is believed to be directly due to 
the particle ingesting capability of alveolar macrophages 
becoming saturated. The data suggest that alveolar macro-
phages become overloaded not by the total mass of ingested 
particles but by the cumulative volume of particles within 
macrophages. Morrow and Mermelstein (1988) estimated 
that overload may occur when the average volumetric load of 
particles exceeds 60 ōm3/macrophage and that macrophage-
mediated clearance ceases when the volumetric load exceeds 
600 ōm3/macrophage. Once the phagocytic activity of mac-
rophages is reduced by large numbers of particles within 
alveoli, the mobility of macrophages also diminishes even if 
the particles are not inherently cytotoxic. The immobilization 
of alveolar macrophages within the lung results in two detri-
mental effects: (1) subsequent clearance is greatly slowed, and 
(2) the macrophages may continue to elaborate bioactive sub-
stances (e.g., proteases, cytokines, growth factors, oxidants, 
and immuno-modulating agents) within the lungs. As a con-
sequence, it is then more likely that a prolonged inflammatory 
response will occur, with attendant lung injury and/or tumor 
development.
The impetus for the overload theory lies, in part, from rodent 
chronic inhalation bioassays with diesel soot (McClellan 
et al. 1985, Mauderly et al. 1987), carbon black (Heinrich 
et al. 1994, Nikula et al. 1995), antimony trioxide (Drew et al. 
1986), toner (Muhle et al. 1991), titanium dioxide (Lee et al. 
1985), and talc (NTP 1993), particles that previously had been 
considered benign and do not ordinarily cause pathologic 
changes if lung clearance processes are not overwhelmed or 
there may be other factors such as hormonal effects enhanc-
ing the inflammatory response (Zhang et al. 2015, Mandarino 
et al. 2020). As a class, these particles have low solubility, 
low cytotoxicity, and are generally non-genotoxic, although 
genotoxicity per se is not a rigid requirement for tumor devel-
opment with these particles (Heinrich et al. 1995). Consistent 
with all, however, is the observation that high levels of dust 
exposure were associated with an increasing lung particle 
burden. At some burden threshold exceeding the capacity 
of alveolar macrophages to remove particles, lung overload 
develops (Warheit et al. 1997).
There are several key features of particle overload. First, 
species differences are known, thereby complicating extrapo-
lation of results from test animals to humans. Rats had more 
severe inflammation after repeated inhalation of carbon black 
or ultrafine TiO2 compared to hamsters or mice (Bermudez 
et al. 2004, Elder et al. 2005). Elder et al. (2005) also noted that 
lung injury and particle burdens with TiO2 tended to increase 
as the surface area of the particles increased. Therefore, a 
second consideration is that local tissue dose and subsequent 
injury from inhaled particles may also be related to particle 
surface area. While other metrics of exposure concentration 
relevant to overload phenomena include particle mass, count, 
volume, or surface area, aggregate surface area appears to be 
the particle characteristic most closely associated with pul-
monary inflammation, fibrosis, and lung tumor development 
(Oberdorster et al. 1994, Castranova 1998, Brown et al. 2001).
By whatever metric, the most important outcome of over-
load is the persistent inflammation produced by the particles. 
The consequences of this have an important bearing on the 
mechanism of tumor development. Marked impairment of 
particle clearance occurred in rats at 7.1 mg/m3 or greater 
with carbon black and was associated with lung injury, 
chronic inflammation, epithelial hyperplasia, and fibrosis 
(Driscoll et al. 1996); such changes were not seen in com-
parably exposed rats at 1.1 mg/m3, suggesting a threshold 
phenomenon. In the overloaded rat lung, the investigators 
reported a significant, dose-dependent increase of hypoxan-
thine-guanine phosphoribyosyltransferase (HPRT) mutations 
in alveolar epithelial cells at inflammatory concentrations. 
Inflammatory cell products, including ROS and cytokines, 
were thought to be causative, including induction of cellular 
mutations and cell proliferation that promote tumor develop-
ment from oxidant-induced genotoxicity. The observation that 
particle-elicited rat inflammatory lung cells can be directly 
mutagenic suggests that there will be an exposure level (or 
lung burden) below which it does not induce persistent inflam-
matory changes and therefore mutations should not occur for 
non-genotoxic particles. However, at inflammatory concen-
trations, both the inherent genotoxicity of the particle and the 
resultant genotoxicity ensuing from the particle-elicited pro-
inflammatory milieu may drive tumor development. Overall, 
the overload theory provides a plausible way that lung tumors 
could develop from heavy exposure to low cytotoxicity, low-
solubility non-genotoxic dusts.
30.6.2  Animal Models
The selection of an animal species for an inhalation study is 
important for practical, technical, and ethical reasons. Clearly, 
no one ideal human surrogate exists; each species has its own 
advantages and disadvantages. Most commonly, rodents, 
including the rat, mouse, guinea pig, and hamster (probably 
in that order), are used. As a consequence of the extensive use 
of rodents in inhalation toxicology, investigators have gener-
ated a large body of information on their responses. Rodents 
have been used in toxicological tests as models for vapor 
particle deposition and clearance, mechanisms of toxicant-
induced lung and airway injury, and as models for infectiv-
ity and immunologic function. Due to their relatively short 
lifespan, the use of rodents is a reasonable approach for study-
ing chronic toxicity and carcinogenesis. When extrapolating 
from experimental animal data to humans, important factors 
include: (1) the comparative anatomy of the respiratory tract, 
(2) the presence or absence of concurrent diseases or infec-
tions, and (3) similarities of the biochemical and physiologic 
responses in the intended species.

1642
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The use of animals in product development and safety assess-
ment needs to continually reflect the principles of good wel-
fare and ethical treatment of animals. Intelligent strategies to 
minimize animal testing by using in vitro or tiered approaches 
to data collection should be practiced, where possible. In vitro 
tests for inhalation-related endpoints are being developed for 
regulatory acceptance, harmonization, and validation. In prac-
tice, inhalation testing should be conducted at laboratories that 
possess appropriate facilities and have a trained, competent 
staff capable of humane animal care and that have institutional 
review committees to assure that their protocols are compliant 
with animal welfare policies. All inhalation studies should be 
scientifically and ethically justified, and every study should be 
designed to avoid or minimize pain, discomfort, and stress. 
Care needs to be taken to avoid unnecessary or duplicative 
testing. Finally, the use of animal enrichment measures, wher-
ever practical, should be encouraged.
In practice, however, selection of the test species is more 
often based on criteria such as the size and availability of the 
test animals, the number of animals needed to discern chemi-
cally induced changes from background or incidental changes, 
and the expense involved in obtaining and maintaining the 
requisite number of animals needed for statistical validity. 
Indeed, techniques to reduce the use of animals, without com-
promising the study, are an important consideration in study 
design.
In the selection of an appropriate animal species, one must 
consider:
•	 the objective of the study,
•	 the available toxicological information,
•	 the unique functional or structural characteristics, if 
any, of the species that make it an ideal (or inappro-
priate) animal model,
•	 what the anticipated response might be that would 
enable the investigator to best determine how many 
animals are needed for the study duration,
•	 appropriate controls.
Historically, the use of small rodents predominates because 
their size allows testing of larger groups, their relatively short 
lifetime allows testing over the entire lifespan, and the cost 
of their acquisition and maintenance is relatively low. Also, a 
large body of data already exists on these species which pro-
vides a ready database useful for planning and comparative 
purposes.
The guinea pig has been used in studies of immune function 
and respiratory sensitization and has been particularly useful 
in determining the relative potencies of isocyanates (Wong and 
Alarie 1982, Weyel and Schaffer 1985). The guinea pig has 
unusually abundant bronchial smooth muscle, which makes 
this species a useful model to study airway hyperresponsive-
ness or bronchoconstriction in asthma models.
The hamster, having a relatively low spontaneous lung 
tumor rate and resistance to pulmonary infections, has been 
used in respiratory tract cancer studies. Some investigators 
feel that the hamster is the best animal model for the study of 
experimental lung cancer (Saffiotti 1970).
There are several disadvantages in using rodents to pre-
dict effects in humans. Because the nasal/pharyngeal anat-
omy of rodents differs considerably from that of humans, 
the amounts and sites of particle deposition may be quite 
different. Rodents are also obligate nose breathers, with 
much better nasal filtering efficiency compared to humans. 
The most serious problems with rodents, especially rats, are 
those involving spontaneous respiratory infections and their 
sequelae. Rats appear to be uniquely susceptible to chronic 
inflammation, pulmonary fibrosis, and cancer from insolu-
ble, noncytotoxic particles by overwhelming lung clearance 
mechanisms (particle overload). Because rats appear most 
susceptible to particle overloading, their selection for some 
types of particle inhalation studies often complicates the 
extrapolation of the results to humans (Oberdorster 1995). 
In oncogenicity studies, rats appear to be less susceptible 
to fiber-induced mesothelioma, a malignant tumor of the 
pleural lung surface. Conversely, hamsters appear to be 
more susceptible to the development of fiber-induced meso-
thelioma but less sensitive to the induction of lung tumors 
than rats. Despite these considerations, rats remain the most 
favored animal model for both short- and long-term inhala-
tion studies.
Inhalation toxicity studies have also been conducted on 
dogs. Many dogs are conveniently sized for several laboratory 
measurements including evaluation of pulmonary and cardiac 
function (Godleski et al. 2000, Wellenius et al. 2003, Bartoli 
et al. 2009a, b). Several natural disease states exist in the dog, 
making it a good model for evaluating the impact of test sub-
stances on conditions such as asthma. The cost and facilities 
needed to properly care for dogs are a disadvantage relative to 
rodents. The dog may also be relatively insensitive to certain 
inhaled gases such as ozone (Stokinger 1957). An advantage 
is that dogs have been used extensively in studying the effects 
of inhaled radioactive materials, thus yielding a wealth of 
historical information concerning the long-term inhalation of 
radionuclides.
Because the nasal anatomy of monkeys is similar to humans, 
monkeys are sometimes used in inhalation experiments. Lung 
function can be determined. However, considerable cost is 
involved in obtaining and maintaining monkey colonies, and 
their lack of general availability is a problem limiting their 
use. Note that within the various species considered monkeys, 
differences do exist. Also, the subgross pulmonary anatomy 
differs between types of monkeys and humans (McLaughlin 
et al. 1961).
Other species, such as the ferret, horse, donkey, sheep, cat, 
rabbit, and pig, have been considered for use in inhalation 
studies. Each of these species has been used for special appli-
cations, which may take advantage of a particular anatomi-
cal feature, chemical sensitivity, or research curiosity. In some 
instances, the endpoint of concern drives the animal model 
chosen. For example, the brown Norway rat has been used as 
an asthma model in a study design including analysis of cel-
lular infiltrate in the lung, inflammatory factors in bronchoal-
veolar lavage, immunoglobulin E product in the serum, and 
changes in delayed-onset respiratory reaction upon inhalation 
challenges (demonstrated with diphenylmethane diisocyanate 
[Pauluhn 2005]).

1643
Inhalation Toxicology
30.6.3  Specific Respiratory Tract Targets 
for Deleterious Responses and 
their Toxicological Assessment
30.6.3.1  Nose and Nasopharyngeal Region
The nasopharyngeal region plays an important role in the 
physiological responses to inhaled irritants. There are three 
basic types of irritants (Alarie 1973): (1) sensory irritants that 
act on the trigeminal nerve, (2) pulmonary irritants that act on 
irritant receptors of the airways, and (3) mixed sensory and 
pulmonary irritants. The initial response to an inhaled irri-
tant may involve an immediate burning or stinging sensation 
in the eyes, nose, and/or throat. Such stimuli may range from 
unpleasant discomfort to extreme pain and are mediated by 
interaction of irritants with chemoreceptive nerve endings 
from the trigeminal nerve in the cornea, nose, tongue, oral 
cavity, and upper respiratory tract. Once stimulated, these 
nerve endings can cause systemic responses, resulting not only 
in a burning sensation of the nose and eyes but also in a reflex 
reduction in respiratory rate. This response occurs once a 
threshold concentration at the irritant receptor is exceeded and 
develops immediately or within a few minutes of exposure and 
is both time- and concentration-dependent. Irritant responses 
are generally protective in nature, limiting further exposure 
to the offending substance, especially to the lower respiratory 
tract. Although the effects of sensory irritants per se are not 
usually life-threatening, irritants have the capacity to cause 
changes in the respiratory tract ranging from minor epithelial 
injury to fatal lung edema hours or days after exposure.
In our environment, there are many different types of respi-
ratory tract irritants. Chlorine, for example, has been used as 
a war gas, and an accidental release in 1984 of methyl isocya-
nate caused massive human exposures in India. Chemical irri-
tants act on specific targets. Formaldehyde acts primarily on 
respiratory epithelial cells, chlorine acts on the nasal cilia and 
bronchial functions, dimethylamine acts on olfactory sensory 
cells, and cigarette smoke affects the laryngeal epithelium. 
Furthermore, a number of disease states, including asthma, 
asphyxiation, chronic bronchitis, emphysema, pulmonary 
fibrosis, and pneumoconiosis, can be induced or exacerbated 
by exposure to irritants. Some of the effects of inhaled materi-
als on the nose are shown in Table 30.7.
As a portal of entry, the nasal passages are a target site for many 
inhaled substances. For the epithelial mucosa in the nose, squa-
mous tissue may be damaged by glutaraldehyde toxicity (Gross 
et al. 1994), transitional epithelial tissue by ozone (Morgan 1991), 
respiratory mucosal tissue by formaldehyde (Morgan et al. 1991), 
and the olfactory region by μ,μ-iminodipropionitrile (Genter et al. 
1992). The effects produced at these locations may be attributed 
to the local dose reaching the site, site-specific tissue susceptibil-
ity, or a combination of these factors.
It should be emphasized that recording the lesion distribu-
tion is only one step in the process of identifying patterns of 
nasal toxicity. Because the distribution of lesions results from 
the interplay of local dose and tissue susceptibility, the rela-
tive influence and relevance of such responses to humans are 
the most important. For example, the airflow-driven local dose 
of highly reactive water-soluble substances, such as formal-
dehyde, can dictate the lesion distribution, but airflow is not 
likely to be a major factor for lesions produced by less water-
soluble substances, such as ozone, chloroform, and methyl bro-
mide (Kimbell et al. 2001).
It has been shown that nasal enzymes acting at localized 
sites are responsible for the conversion of chemicals, such as 
dibasic acid esters and hexamethylphosphoramide, to reactive 
intermediates that cause injury at enzyme-rich cellular loca-
tions (Bogdanffy 1990, Dahl and Hadley 1991). The role of 
local tissue metabolism has important implications in the risk 
assessment of inhaled materials that cause injury solely in the 
nose. For example, Bogdanffy and Valentine (2003) described 
the role of olfactory cell carboxylesterases in the metabolism 
of vinyl acetate in the development of nasal tumors in rats. 
Intracellular acidification from the liberation of acetic acid and 
protons from the metabolism of vinyl acetate and attendant 
cytotoxicity and regenerative hyperplasia of olfactory epithe-
lium at high doses is thought to be a threshold-related phenom-
enon in the mode of action for vinyl acetate and perhaps other 
related esters. For most substances, however, further study is 
needed to elucidate the dosimetry, susceptibility, and mecha-
nisms of action for inhaled substances on nasal tissue, particu-
larly for use in extrapolating animal data to humans.
30.6.3.2  Larynx
The laryngeal region, located between the upper respiratory 
tract and the trachea, is also a potential target tissue of inhaled 
toxicants, as a variety of substances can cause epithelial injury 
to this area. The ventral laryngeal epithelium of rats and mice 
is highly responsive to inhaled materials such as cobalt sulfate 
(Bucher et al. 1990), tobacco smoke (Sagartz et al. 1992), and 
a wide variety of industrial chemicals, pharmaceuticals, and 
aerosol propellants (Gopinath et al. 1987).

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Laryngeal lesions commonly involve degeneration of the 
epithelium with subsequent regeneration, hyperplasia, and 
squamous metaplasia. More severe reactions may show epi-
thelial ulceration with exudation. Recovery or regression from 
induced changes is variable and dependent upon the time scale 
and severity of the initial lesion (Lewis 1991). Specific areas of 
the rodent laryngeal mucosa appear to be sensitive to inhaled 
materials. These sites include the epithelium covering the base 
of the epiglottis, ventral pouches, and the medial surfaces 
of the vocal processes of the arytenoid cartilage. Therefore, 
the detection of induced changes requires an anatomically 
directed and thorough histological examination.
30.6.3.3  Tracheobronchial Airways
The tracheobronchial airways may be the target of a number of 
inhaled gases, particles, and fibers. Toxicity may be directed 
at several possible locations: the mucous layer, the mucocili-
ary apparatus of the airway epithelial cells, the epithelial cells, 
the sensory nerve fibers at the epithelial surface, the basement 
membrane, or the serous and mucous glands in the lamina pro-
pria beneath the epithelium. An example of airway toxicity 
at all of these levels can be seen in animal models of chronic 
bronchitis using very high levels of sulfur dioxide (250 ppm 
over several weeks of exposure) as first described by Reid in 
1963. The chronic bronchitis model in the rat is very similar to 
the human disease. It includes mucus hypersecretion, mucous 
plugging of small airways, mucous gland hyperplasia, gob-
let cell metaplasia (Reid 1963), change in particle deposition 
patterns (Sweeney et al. 1995), airway hyperresponsiveness 
(Shore et al. 1995), and significant acute and chronic inflam-
mation (Farone et al. 1995). Early studies with this model 
showed reversibility of these changes within a few weeks of 
cessation of SO2 exposure (Reid 1963). Similar reversibility of 
changes in chronic bronchitis associated with smoking and its 
cessation has been described in people.
30.6.3.4  Pulmonary Region (Bronchioles and Alveoli)
The bronchiolar–alveolar junction is a particularly sensitive 
site of injury and inflammation with exposure to both particles 
and fibers. Figure 30.16 shows how this region is altered in rats 
exposed to either paper or plant or vehicular emissions. The 
deposition of fibers and particles has been demonstrated at this 
site as well as the increase in inflammation at this site (Brody 
and Hill 1981, Brody et al. 1981, Brody and Roe 1983, Saldiva 
et al. 2002). To show that responding neutrophils are indeed 
increased at this site, morphometric studies of the tissues must 
often be done.
The function of macrophages can be affected by cytotoxic 
airborne substances, such as asbestos, quartz, and heavy met-
als (Kawasaki 2019). This response is not limited to cytotoxic 
particles; for example, the instillation of 4 mg of carbon par-
ticles (considered a nuisance dust by its relative lack of cyto-
toxicity) into the lung of a mouse induces an acute macrophage 
response, with the number of macrophages increasing 10-fold 
(Bowden and Adamson 1978). A spectrum of responses, result-
ing in alterations in macrophage numbers, viability, morphol-
ogy, and changes in the phagocytic capacity, can be produced 
by chemical insult (Gardner 1984). The effects of inhaled 
gases and particles on macrophage functions are important 
because of the function of these cells in homeostasis. Some 
macrophage function studies describe the injurious effects of 
gases on macrophage bactericidal activity. In these models, 
animals first exposed to ozone had higher mortality if subse-
quently exposed to pathogenic bacteria, ostensibly by impair-
ing macrophage phagocytosis and lysosomal enzyme activity 
(Coffin et al. 1968, Goldstein et al. 1978, Gardner 1984).
Patterns of response include acute inflammation with infil-
tration of neutrophils and acute lung injury. Acute lung injury 
at the alveolar level includes damage to pulmonary epithelial 
cells, resulting in atelectasis and the eventual formation of 
hyaline membranes along small airways and alveoli. Hyaline 
membranes assume a distinctive eosinophilic appearance 
under the light microscope and contain fibrin, serum proteins, 
and cell debris exuded from the injury to epithelial cells, alve-
olar capillary endothelial cells, and interstitial cells. When the 
alveolar epithelium regenerates, type 2 epithelial cells (which 
also produce surfactant) serve as the stem cell population that 
proliferates to cover any areas of denuded basement membrane 
and injured type 1 epithelial cells. Figure 30.17 illustrates the 
microscopic appearance of acute lung injury.
FIGURE 30.16  Bronchoalveolar junction of the rats: Bar at 50 ōm in all pictures. (a) Normal rat bronchoalveolar junction. (b) Bronchoalveolar junc-
tion of a rat exposed to secondary power plant emissions with minimal inflammation. (c) Bronchoalveolar junction of a rat exposed to vehicular emis-
sions. Note both epithelial proliferation and inflammatory cells in the alveoli.

Inhalation Toxicology
30.6.3.5  Pulmonary Vasculature
The pulmonary vasculature may be the target of injury from 
ingested or inhaled materials (Batalha et al. 2002). The mono-
crotaline model of acute lung injury affects the pulmonary 
vasculature and ultimately results in pulmonary hypertension 
(White and Rothe 1989, Killingsworth et al. 1997).
30.6.4  Speci c Applications of Inhalation Toxicology
In addition to the toxicological responses described above, a 
number of specific areas of inhalation toxicology research are 
worthy of discussion. Some of these require specific equip-
ment for exposure and the studies are directed toward a spe-
cific outcome.
30.6.4.1  Drug Testing
Inhalational toxicity studies have been important in past eval-
uations of certain pharmaceutical drugs and drug candidates, 
particularly those expected to be nebulized and then inhaled as 
the main route of delivery. In vitro and in vivo approaches (the 
latter including both animals, and humans in clinical trials) 
have been used. Traditionally, the large majority of medicines 
with pulmonary delivery have been used for lung diseases, 
especially asthma and/or chronic obstructive pulmonary dis-
ease (Newman 2018). Systemic applications have included dia-
betes, analgesics, hormone therapy, and organ anti-rejection. 
Gene therapy has been tried through inhalation for inherited 
pulmonary diseases, with variable results (dos Reis et al. 2017, 
Newman 2018). Good reviews are available in the literature 
of the physiologic and clinical principles surrounding the use 
of drug formulations for inhalational routes (Frohlich 2017a, 
Newman 2018).
Key points to consider are: (1) the distinct physiologic and 
PK principles that apply to drug inhalation compared to the 
much more common oral (ingestion) route, or an intravenous 
route; (2) the appealing possibility that inhalational routes of 
drug delivery may provide a fast and relatively direct route, 
with less biochemical modification of the drug (Patton et al. 
2004), and with the ability to bypass food ingestion. Besides 
the obvious possibility of direct toxic effects on the lungs, 
delivery of drugs by inhalation does carry drawbacks: the level 
of drug that can be delivered may be insufficient for some sys-
temic therapies (or require very frequent treatments to achieve 
those levels), and the lung may not adequately clear pharma-
ceutical materials that are not absorbed (Frohlich 2017a; also, 
see elsewhere in this chapter for a discussion on frustrated 
phagocytosis). Another factor is that pre-existing particle 
accumulations or stressors to the lung, such as smoking, could 
themselves impair the benefit of inhaled therapies or disrupt 
their absorption (Kotani et al. 2002, Brown et al. 2007, Hodge 
et al. 2007, Frohlich 2017a).
In preclinical safety studies for formulations for inhalational 
delivery, studies in two species are typical, with one usually 
being rodents (Frohlich 2017a). Because rodents are obligate 
nose breathers, techniques such as intra-tracheal instillation 
or intubation are typically needed to bypass the clearance 
mechanisms of the upper respiratory tract (Frohlich 2014, 
Ribero et al. 2015). Anatomic differences between rodents and 
humans need to be considered (Hofmann et al. 1989a), also see 
discussion elsewhere in this chapter), as well as differences in 
metabolizing enzymes (Carlson 2008).
An example of impactful inhalational drug testing in 
recent years is inhaled cyclosporine as a prophylaxis for 
rejection among lung transplant recipients. In a phase 3 clini-
cal trial, PK analysis of aerosolized cyclosporine showed 
that substantial blood concentrations could be delivered to 
recipients, though quite a bit less than for oral administration 
(Corcoran et al. 2014). Prior to such trails, in vivo experi-
ments with dogs were useful in establishing the safety (i.e., 
no limiting lung or systemic toxicity) and the expected PKs 
of aerosolized cyclosporine at dose levels/kg well in excess of 
those expected to be received by human patients (Niven et al. 
2011). Recent research has focused on developing chemically 
more advanced microspheres or NPs as a delivery system 
that would improve inhaled cyclosporine’s efficacy (Sato 
et al. 2016, Yang et al. 2018); one study used asthmatic rats in 
its evaluation process, with suppression of inflammation as a 
criterion (Yang et al. 2018). ALI cell exposure systems (cov-
ered elsewhere in this chapter) have been used as a means 
of testing liposomal delivery of aerosolized cyclosporine 
(Schmid et al. 2017).
A new area of focus is antimicrobial peptides delivered 
through various types of nanocarrier particles, through an 
inhalational route as a way to treat bacterial lung infections. 
A recent paper described the establishment of an ALI cell 
exposure system to test such a delivery route for antimicro-
bial peptides against human A549 cells, with promising results 
(Ritter et al. 2020). The use of nanocarrier particles in the in 
vivo setting would be subject to lung clearance issues and other 
potential limitations regarding inhalational delivery (e.g., pre-
existing lung impairment) that were earlier described.
FIGURE 30.17  Acute lung injury in a mouse lung. Hyaline membranes 
line most alveoli and remodeled terminal airspaces. The interstitium is 
thickened with inflammatory cells, and increased numbers of alveolar 
macrophages are seen in most alveoli. Bar = 50 ōm.

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
30.6.4.2  Carcinogenesis/Tobacco Smoking Studies
in vivo inhalational toxicology studies have been very impor-
tant in past carcinogenesis studies, including studies of pol-
lution and other airborne toxicants (Nettesheim and Szakal, 
1972, Nettesheim et al. 1975, Nelson 1977). The list of sub-
stances that can cause cancer through airborne mechanisms, 
and thus that have been studied by inhalational toxicology 
studies, is very long. This includes asbestos fibers (Reeves 
et al. 1974), metals, including cadmium, arsenic, chromium, 
and beryllium (Norseth 1988), numerous volatile organic com-
pounds (Kari et al. 1993, Doi et al. 2004, Dragani 2008), and 
of course tobacco smoke (Kendrick et al. 1976, Dalbey et al. 
1980). Such carcinogenesis studies have had enormous benefi-
cial implications for workplace practices, public policy, and 
public health over the last several decades. The historic piv-
otal importance, and consequent influence on daily life, has 
likely been greatest for the many studies showing pronounced 
adverse effects from tobacco smoke, both actively inhaled and 
“secondhand” smoke (Janerich et al. 1990, Warren et al. 2013).
Inhalation studies of known or suspected carcinogens 
require complex containment systems in order to protect 
laboratory personnel and also to guard against environmen-
tal release of carcinogenic agents. Also, such studies using 
rodents which include the development of cancer as an end-
point (in contrast with other endpoints such as inflammation, 
which typically have shorter time frames) may require at 
least a year, and often 2 or 3 years of exposure and observa-
tion (example provided in Gavett et al. 2016). For this reason, 
relatively few laboratories have adequate facilities for such 
studies. A full set of ethical and practical guidelines for the 
use of animals in cancer research is provided by Workman 
et al. (2010), with specific safety discussions by Frazier (2005) 
and Lehner et al. (2008). To model cigarette smoking, special-
ized “smoking machines” are available commercially such as 
the Jaeger-Baumgartner Cigarette Smoking Machine (with 
Human Puff Profile Pump capability) (www.chtechusa.com), 
a fully automatic stand-alone cigarette smoking device usable 
with tobacco-based cigarettes or e-cigarettes.
A full review of inhalational carcinogenesis is beyond the 
scope of this chapter (the reader is referred to the many refer-
ences cited here for more detail), but several key points regard-
ing inhalational carcinogenesis studies and their impacts are 
summarized below.
First, the concept of carcinogenesis as a multistage process 
evolved gradually during the latter half of the 20th century. 
Investigators realized that most cancers causally originated 
from multiple molecular deficits accumulated over time, which 
in turn reflect (1) the multistage, lengthy time course needed 
for most malignancies to become clinically impactful, and (2) 
the different histopathologic lesion stages that occur in many 
organs and which are visible under a microscope. The latter 
reflects a spectrum from normal to hyperplasia/metaplasia to 
dysplasia (i.e., pre-cancerous) to overt malignancy capable of 
local tissue destruction and metastasis. Animal inhalational 
studies have been important in supporting this concept on a 
histopathologic basis. For example, hyperplastic, metaplastic, 
and malignant changes were seen in the upper and lower respi-
ratory tracts of F344 rats exposed to cigarette smoke (Dalbey 
1980). Also, studies of rats exposed to the nasal carcino-
gens phenylglycidyl ether (Lee et al. 1983b) and hexameth-
ylphosphoramide (Lee and Trochimowicz 1982, 1984) showed 
metaplastic and dysplastic changes in the nasal respiratory 
epithelium, again supporting the concept of carcinogenesis 
as a multistage process that is often reflected in histologically 
observable changes.
Second, inhalational in vivo studies have been important 
in showing the ability of many inhaled carcinogens to be 
absorbed by the lung and travel to distant organ sites, where 
they may exert adverse effects, either in original form or in 
chemically modified/metabolized form to which the target 
organs are sensitive. An example was a rodent whole-body 
inhalation study of propylene glycol mono-t-butyl ether, which 
showed that hepatic adenomas and carcinomas developed in 
the rodents at higher exposures, along with non-neoplastic 
renal nephropathy (Doi et al. 2004).
Third, inhalational carcinogenesis studies have been pivotal 
(among many other types of investigations) in showing the 
importance of reactive oxygen species (Vallyathan et al. 1998) 
arising in the setting of inflammation, in mediating the genetic 
damage (e.g., DNA strand breaks, sequence changes, base 
pair modifications, and gene activation or inactivation) from 
inhaled toxicants. Through chemical reactivity which includes 
free radical mechanisms, reactive oxygen species may induce 
lesions in specific growth and regulatory genes that adversely 
impact control over cells’ survival and proliferation. Such 
reactive oxygen species have been observed in the setting of, 
for example, tobacco smoke (Valavanidis et al. 2009), air pol-
lution (Risom 2005), silica (Vallyathan et al. 1998), and iron-
containing particulate dust (Aust et al. 2002). The physical 
and chemical characteristics of inhaled particles are known 
to influence the level of generation of reactive oxygen species 
(Xia et al. 2006, Valavanidis et al. 2009).
Fourth, animal studies, while very important in the evalua-
tion of carcinogenic potential of inhaled substances, not sur-
prisingly have limitations as far as how translatable they are 
to other animal species and to humans, and these should obvi-
ously be considered in the interpretation of such data. Such 
limitations include the presence of clearance mechanisms and 
metabolizing enzymes preferentially found in one species over 
another, anatomic and physiologic respiratory differences 
(previously discussed), and different PKs, distribution path-
ways, and end-organ biochemical sensitivities.
30.6.4.2.1  Electronic Cigarettes
Electronic cigarettes are devices in which liquids of variable 
composition are heated (rather than combusted) with an ele-
ment called an atomizer, producing an aerosol vapor which is 
inhaled as a simulation of, and often a preferred alternative to, 
regular tobacco smoking. The liquid to be vaporized (known 
as an e-liquid) usually contains propylene glycol, glycerin, nic-
otine, flavors and traces of various heavy metals, carcinogens 
and other toxins, and even metal NPs (Hajek et al. 2014, Grana 
et al. 2014, Walley et al. 2019). The exact composition depends 
on the manufacturer; for example, some e-liquids are nicotine-
free. Usage of these devices is typically called vaping. The 
specifics of how these products are used (both the vaping 
action itself, and user-driven customization of the liquid(s) to

1647
Inhalation Toxicology
be atomized) can influence the size and composition of the 
aerosol particles, and thus the site(s) of pulmonary deposition 
(Kim et al. 2016, Lechasseur et al. 2019).
Electronic cigarettes are important to respiratory toxicol-
ogy because their usage has briskly increased within the last 
decade. Factors explaining their popularity include skillful 
marketing, the perception of their being possibly less harmful 
than tobacco smoking, and regular smokers attempting to use 
e-cigarettes as a substitute, or as an incremental step in the 
process of quitting such products altogether.
The risks of electronic cigarettes have been debated, with 
considerable difficulty in reaching a consensus on specific 
health risks (Zhao et al. 2016, Brady et al. 2019). The variability 
of e-liquids and vaping practices likely makes it more difficult 
to statistically relate adverse outcomes to a specific compo-
nent of the atomized mixtures. Nicotine, a strongly addictive 
component of many e-liquids, is of course well-studied from 
its natural presence in tobacco products. However, most of the 
highly deleterious effects from tobacco use, especially car-
cinogenesis, are thought to arise from other substances such 
as tar-based aromatic compounds, rather than nicotine itself. 
It is these tar-based substances that are typically much less 
abundant in e-liquids. The overall perception remains that 
electronic cigarette usage is likely a safer alternative to regular 
tobacco smoking (Burstyn 2014), but much less safe than not 
using such products at all (Centers for Disease Control (CDC) 
2020).
In 2019, the CDC reported an outbreak of severe lung 
injury tied to the practice of vaping, involving hundreds of 
cases (Schier et al. 2019). Case numbers eventually climbed 
into the thousands, then abated somewhat (CDC 2020). The 
symptoms were typically described as similar to pneumonia, 
except that cultures were negative and subjects did not typi-
cally respond to antibiotic therapy (CDC 2020). Symptoms in 
some cases progressed to respiratory failure, requiring inten-
sive care and mechanical ventilation. Pathologic lung exami-
nation revealed organizing pneumonitis and diffuse alveolar 
damage (Mukhopadhyay et al. 2019), which are nonspecific 
injury endpoints with many etiologies, but that are believed 
to be initiated by direct injury to alveolar epithelial cells. The 
CDC found that vitamin E acetate (VEA), a common addi-
tive in cannabinoid-based vaping mixtures, was strongly 
associated with lung injury (CDC 2020). It was found that 
this compound, when vaporized, can produce highly toxic 
ketene (C2H2O) gas, as well as carcinogenic alkenes and 
benzene (Wu and O’Shea 2020). The CDC consequently rec-
ommended strongly that VEA not be added to any vaping 
products (CDC 2020).
Aldehydes produced from the thermal degradation of the 
main humectants in e-liquids may be a source of lung injury 
(Gillman et al. 2020). Flavor additives and metallic com-
pounds are also possible sources of toxicity (Fiani et al. 2020). 
Other substances may also yet emerge as causes of, or associ-
ated with, toxicity and tissue injury in the context of vaping.
In one study, particles generated from electronic cigarettes 
were found to be generally <200 nm particle count median 
diameter, with some size effect based on puffing volume 
and humectant composition; these overall were found to be 
smaller than combustible tobacco-generated aerosol particles, 
and estimated to deposit in both tracheobronchial and alveolar 
regions (Son et al. 2020). Another study found that second-
hand aerosol exposure from electronic cigarettes contained 
nano-sized particles, which the authors regarded as a poten-
tial hazard to bystanders given the propensity of such particles 
to deposit by diffusion in many levels of the respiratory tract 
(Palmisani et al. 2019).
In vitro ALI systems with human bronchial epithelial cells 
have shown that electronic cigarette-generated propylene glycol 
and glycerol aerosol particles cause an increase in proinflam-
matory cytokines IL-6 and IL-8 and in cellular stress markers 
HMOX1 and NQ01 (Escobar et al. 2020). Significantly, e-liq-
uid vapor has been associated with adverse changes in gene 
expression related to carcinogenesis and immune suppression 
(Martin et al. 2016, Tommasi et al. 2019). It seems likely that 
future investigations will continue to reveal additional adverse 
toxicologic pathways associated with electronic cigarette use.
30.6.4.3  Air Pollution/Occupational Studies
In the 1990s, numerous epidemiological studies identified 
associations between increases in ambient particulate air pol-
lution with increases in human mortality and morbidity, spe-
cifically in respiratory and cardiovascular deaths as well as 
hospital admissions. People of advanced age often with pre-
existing pulmonary and cardiovascular disease were identified 
as most susceptible. To assess the plausibility of these findings, 
new approaches to inhalation toxicology studies were needed. 
As described earlier in this chapter, ambient particle concen-
trators and systems to model ambient air chemistry with the 
formation of secondary particles were developed. The use of 
animal models of diseases similar to those found susceptible 
in epidemiological studies was applied. An animal model of 
chronic bronchitis had increased inflammation with concen-
trated ambient particles (CAPs) exposure (Clarke et al. 1999, 
Saldiva et al. 2002). A rat model of myocardial infarction 
showed increased arrhythmias with inhalation of CAPs and 
an ischemia model in dogs showed enhanced ischemic change 
with CAPs exposure (Wellenius et al. 2003, 2004). Secondary 
particles from power plants and traffic sources both showed 
adverse changes in lung and cardiovascular outcomes in ani-
mal studies (Godleski et al. 2011, Wellenius et al. 2011, Diaz 
et al. 2013, Lamoureux et al. 2013). Driven by the epidemiol-
ogy findings, many notable advances in inhalation toxicology 
can be found in these studies including the innovative use of 
indwelling devices to create cardiac ischemia models (Bartoli 
et al. 2009a), the use of telemetry devices for cardiac monitor-
ing in dogs and rodents (Wellenius et al. 2003, 2004, Bartoli 
et al. 2009b, Lamoureux et al. 2013, Carll et al. 2018), and the 
use of whole-body plethysmograph chambers for continuous 
respiratory functional monitoring (Diaz et al. 2011).
Occupational studies have also taken on new “real-world” 
directions. Studies of laser printer emissions have recently 
been done in which a laser printer was placed within a cham-
ber and operated continuously, with airflow passing through 
the chamber (Pirela et al. 2019). The resulting aerosol con-
tained nanotechnology enable particles and was piped for rat 
exposures in whole-body plethysmographs. Control animals 
breathed filtered air. In addition to measuring respiratory

1648
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
parameters, the rats also had implanted telemeters from 
which electrocardiogram and pressure signals were collected 
from the heart. The emitted laser printer particles produced 
increases in cardiovascular risk by augmenting sympathetic 
influence, impairing ventricular performance and repolariza-
tion, and inducing hypertension and arrhythmia. Thus, laser 
printer-emitted particles may cause significant health risks 
through adverse cardiovascular effects, especially in occupa-
tional settings, among susceptible individuals with long-term 
exposure (Pirela et al. 2019, Carll et al. 2020).
30.6.5  Evolving Approaches to the Assessment 
of Pulmonary Responses
Analytic methods available for assessing the toxicology of 
materials reaching the respiratory tract are evolving. The con-
tinuing increase in the creativity and capabilities of science 
and engineering have given rise to an ever-growing number 
of chemicals, particles, and other reagents that have never 
been experienced by the billions of individuals living on our 
planet. For new materials, there can be no epidemiological 
studies in humans. Although considerable information can be 
learned from lifetime studies in animals, these experimental 
approaches are expensive and take many years. Thus, there 
is increasing demand for the development and calibration 
of high-throughput assays, most of which involve biological 
measurements in cultured cells. While many reagents can be 
tested, these assays fail to fully recapitulate the physical and 
humoral interactions provided by the extracellular matrix, 
cell–cell interactions, and the diverse cellular components of 
the actual respiratory tract.
One approach to filling the gap between simple cell assays 
and intact organisms is by using precision-cut lung slices. 
Slices can be made from both animal and human lungs, and 
they function for 7–10 days. This ex vivo approach comes far 
closer to reality than the cell culture systems in wide use. 
A recent description of this technique has been published 
by Rosner et al. (2014). Tice et al. (2013) have reviewed an 
approach called Tox21, which integrates data from a variety 
of technologies and multiple endpoints utilizing techniques 
of systems biology and applying them to toxicology. Geenen 
et al. (2012) review how systems biology can incorporate mul-
tiple sources of information and produce in silico systems 
where systems biology can explore network-mediated toxic-
ity and make predictions about likely toxic responses as well 
as predict candidate biomarkers for injury to the respiratory 
tract.
Finally, as is true for all organizational levels of toxicology 
from isolated cells to humans, there is increased interest in 
genomics. We need to identify susceptibility genes and quan-
tify gene expression elicited by exposures to inhaled chemicals 
and particles. The emphasis inevitably is on gene–environ-
ment interactions. Then, Van Hummelen and Sasaki (2010) 
review the broad field of toxicogenomics and how it has been 
used in environmental toxicology.
Supporting these approaches to hazard identification and 
risk quantification are the fields of proteomics, epigenetics or 
epigenomics, and metabolomics (van Ravenzwaay et al. 2012). 
Examples of this direction are detailed in the next section.
30.6.6  ‘Omics Methods
As detailed in Chapter 7, ‘omic methodologies, such as 
genomics, transcriptomics, proteomics, and lipidomics, are 
important tools in modern toxicologic research. Each of the 
numerous ‘omic disciplines is named for a distinct class of 
molecules studied, but they share a common feature in terms 
of the scale of analysis. Typically, very large numbers of mol-
ecules are measured, revealing the levels of thousands of RNA 
transcripts or proteins in each sample in a study. This can pro-
vide exciting insights into the molecular changes behind toxi-
cologic responses or help identify biomarkers of exposure. The 
‘omics tools also come with unique challenges in statistical 
analysis and experimental design. Key methodologic details 
are provided in Chapter 7. Here we survey their use within 
inhalation toxicology and highlight some instructive examples.
30.6.6.1  Approaches
For inhalation toxicology, ‘omic analyses have provided 
insights across a wide variety of investigations. Typical samples 
are derived from in vitro cell culture models or from subjects 
after in vivo exposures. The latter include both well-controlled 
experimental systems (e.g., animal models or human subjects 
in exposure chambers) or after actual “real-world” inhalation 
of potential or known toxins. One major advantage of respi-
ratory toxicology is the relative ease of sampling the target 
organ by lavage to collect cells and lung lining fluid for analy-
sis. Indeed, lavage samples have also been the focus of many 
‘omic studies of toxic effects of inhaled agents, as illustrated 
in the examples below. However, blood samples are also com-
monly used, especially for studies in human subjects.
The specific objectives of ‘omic studies in respiratory toxi-
cology are broadly similar to those sought by methodologies in 
toxicology. These include biomarker discovery, e.g., molecules 
that reveal past exposure(s) or future susceptibility (especially 
relevant for ‘predictive toxicology’). In a related strategy, ‘omic 
methods can also be used to screen compounds for potential 
toxicity with the objective of reducing or avoiding risk for new 
chemical agents that are developed. In other applications, the 
major goal is to identify the molecular mechanisms for toxic 
effects of inhaled agents. These distinct categories are concep-
tually useful, but in practice, investigators will search ‘omic 
data for results that advance knowledge in any or all of these 
areas.
30.6.6.1.1  Air Pollution
Gene expression profiling using microarray and RNAseq 
methods has been extensively used for studies of air pollution 
effects. In vitro examples include identification of inflamma-
tory and immune response pathways such as MAPK signaling 
and cytokine-cytokine receptor interaction in human bron-
chial epithelial cells exposed to various concentrations of 
particles smaller than 2.5 ōm (PM2.5) collected from Beijing, 
China (Ding et al. 2014, Zhou et al. 2015). In vivo expo-
sure of mice to inhaled ozone followed by RNAseq analysis 
revealed transcriptional responses in two critical pulmonary 
tissue compartments: CA and airway macrophages (Tovar 
et al. 2020), with notable differences in these two distinct lung

1649
Inhalation Toxicology
components. In airways genes associated with epithelial barrier 
function, detoxification processes, and cellular proliferation 
were altered, while in alveolar macrophages ozone affected 
genes involved in innate immune signaling, cytokine produc-
tion, and extracellular matrix remodeling. In human subjects, 
ozone exposure caused a dose-dependent up-regulation of sev-
eral similar biologic pathways involved in inflammation and 
repair including chemokine and cytokine secretion, activity, 
and receptor binding; metalloproteinase and endopeptidase 
activity. Asthmatic subjects had 1.7- to 3.8-fold higher expres-
sion of many differentially expressed genes, suggesting a link 
between increased proinflammatory and matrix degradation 
and remodeling signals and the harmful effects of elevated 
ozone levels on this group (Leroy et al. 2015).
Other ‘omic modalities have also contributed to air pollutant 
studies. Since diesel exhaust (DE) exposure promotes allergic 
responses, investigators have conducted a proteomics-based 
study of how inhaled DE affects allergen-induced proteins in 
the lung. Lavage samples from control as well as DE/allergen 
exposed subjects were studied using two-dimensional liquid 
chromatography–tandem mass spectrometry. From a total of 
1,894 proteins quantified, 40 proteins were unique to DE expo-
sure alone, while six were linked to the combination of DE and 
allergen, and revealed novel inflammatory roles for the pro-
teins cystatin 1 and 2 (Mookherjee et al. 2018). Metabolomics 
study of lung lavage fluid after inhalation of biodiesel exhaust 
showed distinct profiles in bronchial wash compared to deeper 
lung lavage and found shifts in a number of metabolites that 
may prove useful as biomarkers (e.g., 1-monostearylglycerol, 
sucrose, inosine, nonanoic acid, and ethanolamine) (Surowiec 
et al. 2016).
30.6.6.1.2  Vaping-associated Lung Injury
The 2019 outbreak of e-cig or vaping product use-associated 
acute lung injury (EVALI) increased interest in the inhala-
tional toxicology of these agents. Earlier transcriptome stud-
ies identified distinct gene expression programs in bronchial 
epithelial cells exposed in vitro (Moses et al. 2017) as well 
in the same cells from e-cigarette smokers (Corbett et al. 
2019). A potential hazard was suggested by decreased expres-
sion of a large number of immune-related genes, consistent 
with immune suppression at the level of the nasal mucosa 
(Martin et al. 2016). Lipidomics-based investigations in mice 
exposed to delivery vehicles used in e-cigarette formulations 
(vegetable glycerin and propylene glycol (Szafran et al. 2020) 
or VEA (Muthumalage et al. 2020) also found altered levels 
of lipid immunoregulatory mediators. The strong associa-
tion of EVALI with vaping of tetrahydrocannabinol (THC)-
containing products focused attention on VEA, used in many 
of the devices to dilute THC oil or nicotine-containing vap-
ing liquids. In addition to showing that VEA exposures in 
mice caused dose-dependent increases in lung injury markers 
(fluid leak, cellular inflammation), RNAseq profiling revealed 
extensive differential gene expression between VEA-exposed 
and control (unexposed) alveolar type II cells identified, with 
enrichment in the IL-17, mitogen-activated protein kinase 
(MAPK), and TNF signaling pathways. The findings illustrate 
the potential of ‘omic methodologies to rapidly inform under-
standing of novel inhalational toxins.
30.6.6.1.3  Nanomaterials
Nano-sized materials (<100-nm diameter) are increasingly 
found in products used in healthcare, cosmetics, and medi-
cine. Beginning with the recognition that inhaled ultrafine 
(nanoscale) particles can cause lung toxicity (Oberdorster 
2000), investigators have identified a range of adverse effects. 
The potential toxicity of metal, metal oxide, and carbon-based 
NPs has drawn the most attention because exposure to these 
types of NPs is highest, can occur over long periods, and clear-
ance of such particles is slow. A rich literature using classic 
cell-based in vitro assays and animal models has revealed key 
aspects of NP effects, emphasizing the mechanistic role of cel-
lular oxidative stress and particle size as determinants of tox-
icity (Frohlich 2017b). Transcriptome profiling has helped to 
identify stress response, DNA damage, and metabolic changes 
linked to matching phenotypic changes in vitro as well as 
injury patterns in vivo (Frohlich 2017b). Similar results point-
ing to oxidative stress have been found using metabolomic 
approaches (Bannuscher et al. 2020). These efforts include 
promising attempts in animal models to identify predictive 
biomarkers in whole blood after lung exposures (Khaliullin 
et al. 2020).
An important recent development has been an National 
Institute of Environmental Health Sciences (NIEHS)-
sponsored drive for the analysis of identical libraries of well-
defined ENMs by different laboratories to increase the power 
and reproducibility of findings (Thrall and Nadadur 2020). 
Indeed, using one such library quantitative cellular proteome 
profiles were found to distinguish the cytotoxic potential of 
nanomaterial types based on their induction of pathways 
involved in the cellular antioxidant response, TCA cycle, 
oxidative stress, endoplasmic reticulum stress, and immune 
responses as major processes impacted (Zhang et al. 2020).
30.6.6.2  ’Omics Challenges
These powerful methodologies come with some challenges 
and limitations that merit consideration (Federico et al. 2020, 
Kinaret et al. 2020). The statistical analysis of ‘high-dimen-
sional’ data is well-known to require correction for false dis-
covery rates, but optimal approaches to data normalization, or 
for evaluation of time-series or dose–response studies of toxi-
cologic interest remain topics of ongoing development. Newer 
single-cell RNAseq or proteomic methods will undoubtedly 
also be applied to inhalation toxicology questions, and come 
with unique statistical analysis issues (Lähnemann et al. 2020). 
The substantial variability in gene expression in human sub-
jects can obscure signals more easily seen when data are ana-
lyzed at the level of gene modules or pathways, but the curation 
of gene annotation required for accurate ontology and pathway 
databases is incomplete at best (Haynes et al. 2018). Finally, 
the value of candidate biomarkers or putative mechanism(s) 
identified through ‘omic profiling requires appropriate valida-
tion. The first and perhaps simplest step in validation is con-
firmation of differential expression using another method, e.g., 
quantitative PCR or specific protein assays. More difficult, but 
ultimately the most valuable is confirmation of predictive value 
in well-designed studies of exposed and control populations.

1650
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
30.7  Conclusion
This chapter has discussed the methods and the scientific basis 
of inhalation toxicology. It describes devices and facilities 
needed to expose animals to inhaled particles and gases, as 
well as approaches to estimating or measuring the fraction of 
the inhaled material that is retained in the respiratory tract. 
We review the ever-growing repertoire of techniques that can 
be used to measure the responses elicited by exposures to toxic 
materials in inspired air. Consequences of exposure to inhaled 
particles and gases can be studied at many different organiza-
tional levels. An understanding of the chemical reactions that 
take place is important, so are cellular effects and a growing 
number of in vitro assays. Controlled exposures, such as intra-
tracheal instillation and insufflation, can be valuable, and so 
can acute and chronic exposures of whole animals. Data from 
humans are useful, both through experimental clinical stud-
ies and observational epidemiologic studies. A challenge is to 
integrate information from these varied sources.
Inhalation toxicology is of growing importance in the 
United States and globally. Increases in population and espe-
cially energy consumption per capita have increased the num-
ber of individuals being exposed to toxic particles and gases, 
as well as the consequences of those exposures. In poor, rural 
areas, there continue to be major concerns about indoor air 
pollution, especially from poorly ventilated sources of heat 
for warmth and cooking. This is particularly a problem for 
women and children. Moreover, the massive migration from 
rural to urban areas has increased the number of individuals 
exposed to significant levels of air pollution from mobile and 
rural sources. We see more clearly increases in morbidity and 
mortality, which are attributable to poor air quality.
Toxic exposures persist in a variety of work environments. 
In the United States, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease 
increases in importance. Much of the rising incidence of respi-
ratory diseases is attributable to the inhalation of particles and 
gases in urban and work environments, and especially to the 
use of tobacco products. Pulmonary diseases are of concern 
because they are difficult to treat and because they typically 
manifest themselves only after decades of apparently innocu-
ous exposure. Emphysema and lung cancer are two compelling 
examples. In their early stages, diagnosis is difficult. In their 
end stages, they stand out as diseases that are difficult, some-
times impossible, to treat. This makes primary prevention of 
toxic exposures critical, and the science of inhalation toxicol-
ogy in both experimental and observational studies essential.
The study of the toxic effects of inhaled chemicals is usually 
more challenging compared to dermal or oral routes of expo-
sure due to the high level of technical expertise necessary for 
the safe generation, control, and characterization of test atmo-
spheres. This chapter provided insights into the toxicological 
properties of inhaled materials and respiratory tract responses, 
as well as descriptions of the basic technology needed to prop-
erly conduct inhalation exposures. Textbook guidance (such as 
that provided by this chapter) is valuable but is no substitute 
for hands-on experience. Inhalation studies are both art and 
science.
Despite the introduction of such novel materials as NPs, 
a foundation in basic inhalation toxicology concepts is 
necessary to evaluate the hazards that these and all other 
new materials pose. The mark of a well-conducted inhala-
tion study is clarity. Details of the test atmosphere genera-
tion, composition, concentration, and uniformity must be 
addressed in order to fully understand the significance of the 
experimental results. Progress creates new materials, new 
opportunities for exposure, and a deeper understanding of 
biological responses. Nevertheless, careful planning and exe-
cution of experimental studies will inevitably produce more 
valuable data.
30.8  Questions
	 30.1  For animals or humans, how do we relate exposure 
concentrations of inhaled aerosols to deposited 
dose? What are the key factors?
	 30.2  Why are there concerns about the potential con-
sequences of nanomaterials, especially particles 
smaller than 20 nm and fibrous materials like 
CNTs?
	 30.3  Discuss polydisperse aerosols and explain why this 
distribution of size will be different depending on 
whether particles are counted, weighed, or charac-
terized by their surface area? For a sphere, how is 
particle size related to mass and surface area?
	 30.4  What factors determine where inhaled gases are 
absorbed? For example, discuss the site of uptake 
and the site of injury for formaldehyde, sulfur diox-
ide, ozone, and carbon monoxide.
	 30.5  What considerations should be given to assess the 
inhalation hazard of a fibrous material intended to 
replace asbestos? What properties are associated 
with increased risk?
	 30.6  Discuss the relative merits of (1) longitudinal epi-
demiologic studies in humans exposed to particles, 
(2) chronic inhalation studies in animals, (3) acute 
in vivo bioassays in animals, and (4) an increasing 
array of in vitro tests.
	 30.7  Imagine marching down the respiratory tract from 
the mouth to the large airways to the small airways, 
and finally to the parenchyma. Now describe how 
geometry and linear velocities will help determine 
the relative efficiency of different deposition mech-
anisms. What are the major deposition mecha-
nisms and which will dominate in these different 
anatomic regions?
	 30.8  Discuss major ways of generating aerosols for 
inhalation toxicologic experiments.
	 30.9  Describe the important principles and issues 
regarding the use of in vitro approaches in inha-
lational toxicology studies, and the key categories 
of such techniques and their respective applications 
and caveats.

1651
Inhalation Toxicology
	 30.10  What are three major ways in which inhala-
tion toxicology studies have contributed to an 
improved understanding of carcinogenesis, and 
one major limitation of animal inhalation toxicol-
ogy studies for this purpose?
	 30.11  What are some of the inhalation toxicity prin-
ciples and issues which inform topics of current 
importance (therapeutic inhalational drug deliv-
ery, electronic cigarettes, and air pollution)?
	 30.12  What are the different fields of ‘omics technology, 
and what are some important present and future 
contributions of such technologies to inhalation 
toxicology?
